The Reality of Allah's Names Before Creation
The commentator affirms the reality of Allah's attributes before creation and refutes doubts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why applying names like 'Creator' to Allah before creation is considered real (ḥaqīqah) and not metaphorical, and why denying this is objectionable.

Core Idea
Allah's names like 'Creator' are real even before creation, not just metaphors.

This passage from Imam Abū Ḥanīfa's creed clarifies a subtle semantic issue: can we truly call Allah 'Creator' before He created anything? The commentator defends this usage as real (ḥaqīqah) and explains why saying otherwise is unacceptable in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Read the opening statement and note the claim: Allah was Creator before creating. This seems counterintuitive in human language but is a theological necessity.
Imam Abū Ḥanīfa repeated this point to emphasize its importance.
2
Understand the semantic principle
The commentator explains that applying a derived name before the existence of its derived meaning is permissible and is real (ḥaqīqah) not metaphorical.
Al-Zarkashī provides the key argument.
3
Examine the refutation of an objection
An objection is raised that saying 'He brought the created into existence in eternity' would imply the pre-eternality of creation. The commentator shows this objection is invalid because the phrase itself indicates creation in time.
4
Reflect on the importance
This belief is not just for scholars but for all Muslims; it safeguards Allah's eternal attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رؤية الله في الآخرة
وكان الله تعالى خالقا قبل أن يخلق، ورازقا قبل أن يرزق، والله تعالى يُرى في الآخرة ..
(وكان الله تعالى خالقا قبل أن يخلق) أي يحدث المخلوق ورازقا قبل أن يرزق) أي يوجد المرزوق فهما من قبيل إطلاق المشتق قبل وجود المعنى المشتق منه، ولعل الإمام الأعظم رحمه الله كرّر هذا المرام للأنام للإعلام بأن هذا المعتقد الصحيح الذي يجب أن يعتمده الخواص والعوام.
وقال الزركشي : إطلاق نحو الخالق والرازق في وصفه سبحانه قبل وجود الخلق والرزق حقيقة وإن قلنا صفات الفعل حادثة، وأيضاً لو كان مجازا لصح نفيه، والحال أن القول بأنه ليس خالقا ورازقا وقادرا في الأزل أمر مستهجن لا يقال مثله ولا يصح دفعه بأنه لا يقال أوجد المخلوق في الأزل حقيقة؛ لأنه يؤدي إلى قدم المخلوق، فإن الفرق بينهما بين، بل قوله أوجد المخلوق إلى آخره بنفسه دليل بين، حيث يشير إلى حدوثه، إلا أنه غير واقع في محله.
والله تعالى يُرى بصيغة المجهول، أي ينظر إليه بعين البصر (في الآخرة) أي القيامة يوم لقوله تعالى: وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَئِذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ إِلَىٰ رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ [القيامة: ٢٢-٢٣]، أي تراه عيانا بلا كيفية ولا جهة ولا ثبوت مسافة، ومن يرى ربه لا يلتفت إلى غيره. ولقوله تعالى: كَلَّا إِنَّهُمْ عَنْ رَبِّهِمْ يَوْمَئِذٍ لَمَحْجُوبُونَ [المطففين: ١٥]، أي ممنوعون، أي بخلاف الأبرار والمؤمنين فإنهم في نظر ربهم مقربون، ولقوله صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم كما في الصحيحين وغيرهما: ((إنكم سترون ربكم كما ترون القمر ليلة البدر، لا تضامون في رؤيته))
The vision of Allah in the Hereafter. And Allah Almighty was Creator before He created, and Provider before He provided, and Allah Almighty is seen in the Hereafter. (And Allah Almighty was Creator before He created) meaning He brings the creature into existence, and Provider before He provided) meaning He brings the provided thing into existence; these are of the type of applying the derived term before the existence of the meaning derived from it. Perhaps Imam al-Aʿẓam (may Allah have mercy on him) repeated this intent to the people to inform that this correct belief must be adopted by the elite and the commoners. And al-Zarkashī said: Applying terms like 'the Creator' and 'the Provider' in describing Him, glory be to Him, before the existence of creation and provision is real (ḥaqīqah) even if we say that attributes of action are originated; also if it were metaphorical (majāz), its negation would be valid, but the case is that saying that He is not Creator, Provider, and Powerful in eternity is something objectionable that is not said, and it cannot be refuted by saying that it is not said 'He brought the created thing into existence in eternity' in reality, because it leads to the pre-eternality of the created; for the difference between them is clear; rather his saying 'He brought the created thing into existence' etc. in itself is a clear evidence, as it indicates its temporal origination, except that it is not in its proper place. And Allah Almighty is seen in the passive voice, i.e., will be looked at with the eye of vision (in the Hereafter) i.e., the Day of Resurrection, due to His saying: 'Faces on that Day are radiant, looking at their Lord' (al-Qiyāmah: 22-23), i.e., they see Him face to face without how (kayfiyyah), without direction (jihah), and without establishing distance; and whoever sees his Lord does not turn to others. And due to His saying: 'No indeed, they (the disbelievers) on that Day will be veiled from their Lord' (al-Muṭaffifīn: 15), i.e., prevented, i.e., unlike the righteous and believers, for they are near their Lord. And due to his saying, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, as in the two Ṣaḥīḥs and others: 'You will see your Lord as you see the moon on the night of the full moon, you will not be harmed in seeing Him.'
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal exposition on two related topics: the attribution of names like 'Creator' to Allah before creation, and the vision of Allah in the afterlife. The commentator begins by quoting a statement from Imam Abū Ḥanīfa (al-Imām al-Aʿẓam) that Allah was Creator before creating and Provider before providing. This is explained as applying a derived name (ishtiqāq) to Allah before the existence of the thing from which the name is derived, a nuance in Arabic semantics. The commentator notes that Imam Abū Ḥanīfa repeated this point to emphasize that it is a correct belief for both elite and commoners. Then al-Zarkashī is cited to defend this usage as real (ḥaqīqah) rather than metaphorical (majāz): if it were metaphorical, negating it would be permissible, but negating that Allah is Creator in eternity is repugnant. An objection that saying 'He brought the created into existence in eternity' would imply the pre-eternality of the created is refuted by noting the clear difference between the two expressions; the expression 'existed the created' itself implies temporal origination. The passage then moves to the vision of Allah in the Hereafter. It affirms that Allah is seen with the eyes (biʿayn al-baṣr) on the Day of Resurrection, based on Quranic verses (al-Qiyāmah 22-23) which describe faces looking at their Lord. The commentator specifies that this vision is without modality (kayfiyyah), without direction (jihah), and without establishing distance (thubūt masāfah). The verse from al-Muṭaffifīn (15) is cited to show that disbelievers are veiled from seeing their Lord, implying that believers see Him. Finally, the well-known ḥadīth from the two Ṣaḥīḥs is quoted, comparing the vision to seeing the full moon, with the clarification that believers will not be harmed (lā tuḍāmmūn) in seeing Him.

Key Terms
المشتق
حقيقة
مجاز
صفات الفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that calling Allah 'Creator' before creation is merely metaphorical or figurative language.
Correction: Al-Zarkashī and the commentator affirm it is real (ḥaqīqah) because if it were metaphorical, negating it would be permissible, but negating that Allah is Creator in eternity is objectionable.

Checkpoint
According to al-Zarkashī, why is it not permissible to say that Allah is not the Creator in eternity?
A) Because it would imply that creation is eternal.
B) Because it is a metaphorical statement that cannot be negated.
C) Because saying 'He is not Creator in eternity' is considered objectionable and not said.
D) Because it would contradict the Quranic verse about vision.

Key Takeaway
Applying names like 'Creator' to Allah before creation is real (ḥaqīqah) and denying it is against proper Islamic belief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah has always been the Creator, even before you existed? How does that reality affect the way you rely on Him for your daily needs?
?
When you say 'Allah is the Provider', is it just a phrase you repeat, or does it carry the weight of His eternal, real action? What step can you take this week to live in the light of that truth?
?
If denying that Allah is 'Creator' in eternity is repugnant, how seriously do you guard your own thoughts and words about Allah's attributes? Are there times you dismiss His names as mere metaphors? What will you change?

The Vision of Allah in the Hereafter
The commentator affirms the vision of Allah in the afterlife with scriptural evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the vision of Allah in the Hereafter is a real, physical sight but without modality, direction, or distance, and that it is confirmed by the Quran and Sunnah.

Core Idea
Believers will see Allah in the Hereafter with their eyes, but without how or direction.

After establishing the reality of Allah's names, the commentator turns to a central eschatological belief: the vision of Allah (ru'yah) in the afterlife. This passage affirms that believers will see their Lord on the Day of Resurrection, while disbelievers are veiled. The vision is real but transcends human understanding of sight.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic proof
Notice the verse 'Faces on that Day are radiant, looking at their Lord' (al-Qiyamah 22-23). This is the primary evidence. The commentator explains it means they see Him face to face.
The vision is described as without how (kayfiyyah), direction (jihah), or distance.
2
Understand the contrast for disbelievers
The verse from al-Mutaffifin (15) shows that disbelievers are veiled from seeing their Lord, implying that believers will see Him.
3
Examine the hadith
The famous hadith comparing seeing Allah to seeing the full moon reinforces both the reality and the ease of vision.
The phrase 'you will not be harmed in seeing Him' indicates no difficulty.
4
Reflect on the theological safeguards
The description 'without how, without direction, without distance' ensures we do not anthropomorphize Allah while affirming a real vision.
This balances transcendence and immanence.

According to the passage, how is the vision of Allah described in terms of modality, direction, and distance?
A) With modality, direction, and distance.

The vision of Allah in the Hereafter is real and physical, yet transcendent, as confirmed by Quran and Sunnah.

?
If you truly believed that you will see Allah with your own eyes on the Day of Resurrection, without any veil, how would that hope change the way you face your sins today? What is one sin you would immediately stop repeating because you long for that vision?
?
The disbelievers will be veiled from their Lord. But what about you—are there any veils of distraction, pride, or disobedience that currently block your heart from seeing Him in this life? What is the one veil you know you must remove before that Day?
?
The hadith says you will see your Lord as clearly as the full moon. But the scholars add: without direction or distance. Does your heart actually desire to see Allah, or are you more attached to the created things you can picture? Make a commitment today to ask Allah for that vision every night in your duʿā.

Allah's Eternal Attributes of Creation and Provision
The commentator explains the real application of 'Creator' and 'Provider' before creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah's attributes of Creator and Provider are real and eternal, applying before the existence of creation, and why this is a necessary belief.

Core Idea
Allah was Creator before creating — a real eternal attribute, not a metaphor.

This lesson examines a key creedal statement from the Fiqh al-Akbar tradition: that Allah is described as Creator and Provider before the existence of any created things. The commentator clarifies that this is not a metaphorical usage but a real description, and he presents a powerful argument from Al-Zarkashi to defend this belief. By understanding this, you will grasp an essential principle about Allah's eternal attributes of action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the opening creedal statement and the commentary that follows.
2
Understand Al-Zarkashi's argument
Identify Al-Zarkashi's reasoning: if the terms were metaphorical, negating them would be possible, but negating eternal creation is repulsive. Therefore, the application must be real.
Consider the principle: 'If it were metaphorical, its negation would be valid.'
3
Reflect on the implication
Why does applying 'Creator' before creation not imply pre-eternity of the creature? The commentator distinguishes between the two, showing that the phrase itself indicates temporality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكان الله تعالى خالقا قبل أن يخلق، ورازقا قبل أن يرزق، والله تعالى يُرى في الآخرة .. (وكان الله تعالى خالقا قبل أن يخلق) أي يحدث المخلوق (ورازقا قبل أن يرزق) أي يوجد المرزوق فهما من قبيل إطلاق المشتق قبل وجود المعنى المشتق منه، ولعل الإمام الأعظم رحمه الله كرّر هذا المرام للأنام للإعلام بأن هذا المعتقد الصحيح الذي يجب أن يعتمده الخواص والعوام. وقال الزركشي : إطلاق نحو الخالق والرازق في وصفه سبحانه قبل وجود الخلق والرزق حقيقة وإن قلنا صفات الفعل حادثة، وأيضاً لو كان مجازا لصح نفيه، والحال أن القول بأنه ليس خالقا ورازقا وقادرا في الأزل أمر مستهجن لا يقال مثله ولا يصح دفعه بأنه لا يقال أوجد المخلوق في الأزل حقيقة؛ لأنه يؤدي إلى قدم المخلوق، فإن الفرق بينهما بين، بل قوله أوجد المخلوق إلى آخره بنفسه دليل بين، حيث يشير إلى حدوثه، إلا أنه غير واقع في محله.
And Allah Almighty was Creator before creating, and Provider before providing, and Allah Almighty will be seen in the Hereafter. (And Allah Almighty was Creator before creating) meaning He produces the created thing, and (Provider before providing) meaning He brings into existence the provided thing. Both of these fall under the category of applying a derived term before the existence of the meaning from which it is derived. Perhaps the Great Imam, may Allah have mercy on him, repeated this intent to the people to inform them that this correct belief must be adopted by the elite and the common. Al-Zarkashi said: The application of such terms as Creator and Provider as descriptions of Him, exalted, before the existence of creation and provision is real (haqiqah), even if we say that the attributes of action are originated. Moreover, if it were metaphorical, its negation would be valid, and the statement that He is not Creator, Provider, and Powerful in pre-eternity is a repulsive matter that is not said, nor can it be refuted by saying that it is not said that He brought the created thing into existence in pre-eternity in reality, because that leads to the pre-eternity of the created thing. Indeed, the difference between the two is clear. Rather, His statement 'brought the created thing into existence' etc. itself is a clear proof, indicating its temporality, except that it does not apply in its place.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the creedal statement about Allah's eternal attributes of creation and provision. It begins by quoting the creed: 'Allah was Creator before creating, Provider before providing...' The commentator then explains that these terms are derived (mushtaqq) and are applied to Allah before the existence of the objects (the created things). This is not metaphorical but real (haqiqah). The Great Imam (Abu Hanifa) repeated this to emphasize that it is a correct belief for all, elite and common. Al-Zarkashi is then cited to strengthen the point: calling Allah 'Creator' before creation is real, even if we hold that attributes of action are originated (haditha). If it were metaphorical, it could be negated, but negating eternal creation/provision is repulsive. The objection that saying 'He brought the created thing into existence in pre-eternity' would imply pre-eternity of the creature is refuted: the difference is clear, and the phrase itself shows temporality.

خالق
رازق
مشتق
حقيقة
صفات الفعل
مجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'Creator' only applies after creation, implying it is not an eternal attribute.
Correction: The passage affirms that Allah is truly called Creator before creation, and this is real, not metaphorical.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is applying 'Creator' to Allah before creation considered real (haqiqah) rather than metaphorical?
A) Because the Quran uses it.
B) Because if it were metaphorical, its negation would be valid, but negating eternal creation is repulsive.
C) Because the Prophet said so.
D) Because the Salaf agreed on it.

Key Takeaway
Allah's attributes of Creator and Provider are real and eternal, applying before the existence of their objects, as affirmed by the creed and defended by rational argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah was your Provider even before you were born? How does that reality affect your anxiety about your future provision today?
?
If Allah is Creator from eternity, what does that say about the permanence of His creative power? Are you living as if His power is only related to the past, or do you expect His creative act in your life today?
?
The passage says negating Allah's eternal attribute of creation is 'repulsive'. Is there any area of your belief where you subtly deny or limit Allah's attributes to only what you can see? How will you correct that?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
Believers Will See Allah in Paradise without Likening or Modality
Affirms beatific vision with restrictions and scriptural proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni doctrine of beatific vision: that believers will see Allah in Paradise with their physical eyes, but in a manner completely unlike any created vision, free from likeness, modality, quantity, and distance.

Core Idea
The beatific vision is real but utterly transcendent: we see Allah without knowing how.

This passage from the sharh establishes one of the most discussed doctrines in Sunni kalam: that the believers will see Allah in Paradise. The author immediately qualifies this vision with a series of negations to safeguard divine transcendence. He then provides scriptural evidence from hadith and Quran, and finally clarifies the relationship between vision and comprehension.

Guided Path
1
Read the doctrinal statement
Focus on the first sentence and note all the restrictions placed on the vision: without likeness, modality, quantity, distance, crowding.
These restrictions are meant to avoid anthropomorphism while affirming the reality of vision.
2
Examine the hadith evidence
Identify the two hadiths cited: the short one about no crowding/harm, and the longer one from Muslim about the veil being lifted.
The longer hadith is explicitly linked to the Quranic verse about 'the best reward and more'.
3
Understand the commentary on negations
The author explains the terms 'without likeness', 'without modality', etc., and adds that there is no distance, connection, separation, indwelling, or union.
This refutes those who claim union with God (ittihad).
4
Grasp the principle of affirmation and negation
The author states a key methodological principle: we affirm what revelation establishes and negate what reason declares Allah transcendent of.
This is the balanced Sunni approach: tathbit and tanzih together.
5
Analyze the Quranic proof from al-An'am 103
Note how the verse 'Eyesight does not comprehend Him' is used to show that vision (ru'yah) is possible while comprehensive perception (idrak) is not.
This refutes those who claim the verse prohibits any vision.

ويراه المؤمنون وهم في الجنة بأعين رؤوسهم بلا تشبيه ولا كيفية ولا كمية ولا يكون بينه وبين خلقه مسافة. لا تضامون في رؤيته. وفي رواية: لا تضارون. وهو حديث مشهور، في الصحيحين وغيرهما مذكور. وقد رواه أحد وعشرون من أكابر الصحابة. ويراه المؤمنون وهم في الجنة بأعين رؤوسهم لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام على ما رواه مسلم: إذا دخل أهل الجنة الجنة يقول الله تبارك وتعالى: تريدون شيئا أزيدكم؟ فيقولون: ألم تبيض وجوهنا، ألم تدخلنا الجنة وتنجينا من النار؟ قال فيرفع الحجاب، أي عن وجوه أهل الجنة، فينظرون إلى وجه الله سبحانه، فما أعطوا شيئا أحب إليهم من النظر إلى ربهم، ثم تلا قوله تعالى: {لِلَّذِينَ أَحْسَنُوا الْحُسْنَى} أي الجنة العليا {وَزِيَادَةٌ} [يونس: 26]، أي النظر إلى وجه المولى. وهو قول الأكثر من السلف بلا تشبيه أي رؤية مقرونة بتنزيه لا مكنونة بتشبيه خلقه ولا كيفية أي في الصورة ولا كميّة أي في الهيئة المنظورة، ولا يكون بينه وبين مسافة أي لا في غاية من القرب ولا في نهاية من البعد، ولا بوصف الاتصال ولا بنعت الانفصال ولا بالحلول والاتحاد كما يقوله الوجودية المائلون إلى الاتحاد. فذات رؤيته ثابت بالكتاب والسنة إلا أنها متشابهة من حيث الجهة والكمية والكيفية فنثبت ما أثبته النقل وننفي عنه ما نزهه العقل، كما أشار إلى هذا المعنى قوله تعالى: {لا تُدْرِكُهُ الأَبْصَارُ} [الأنعام: 103]، أي لا تحيط به الأبصار في مقام الإبصار، فإن الإدراك أخص من الرؤية.
And the believers will see Him while they are in Paradise with the eyes of their heads, without likeness, without modality, without quantity, and there is no distance between Him and His creation. 'You will not be crowded in seeing Him.' And in a narration: 'You will not be harmed in seeing Him.' And it is a well-known hadith, mentioned in the Sahihs and elsewhere. And twenty-one of the senior Companions narrated it. And the believers will see Him while they are in Paradise with the eyes of their heads, because of his saying, peace and blessings be upon him, as related by Muslim: 'When the people of Paradise enter Paradise, Allah, blessed and exalted, says: Do you want anything more? They say: Did You not make our faces white? Did You not admit us to Paradise and save us from the Fire? He said: Then the veil is lifted, i.e. from the faces of the people of Paradise, and they look upon the Face of Allah, glory be to Him, and they have not been given anything more beloved to them than looking at their Lord. Then he recited the saying of the Most High: {For those who have done good is the best (reward)} i.e. the highest Paradise {and more} [Yunus: 26], i.e. looking at the Face of the Master. And it is the saying of the majority of the Salaf: without likeness, i.e. a vision accompanied by transcendence, not enclosed in the likeness of His creation, nor modality, i.e. in form, nor quantity, i.e. in the visible appearance, and there is no distance between Him and it, i.e. not in the extreme of nearness nor in the extreme of farness, nor with the description of connection, nor with the attribute of separation, nor by indwelling or union, as held by the existentialists inclined to unionism. So the essence of seeing Him is established by the Book and the Sunnah, but it is ambiguous with regard to direction, quantity, and modality; so we affirm what the transmitted proof has affirmed and negate from it what reason has declared Him transcendent of, as alluded to by the saying of the Most High: {Eyesight does not comprehend Him} [al-An'am: 103], i.e. eyesight does not encompass Him in the mode of seeing, for comprehension (idrak) is more specific than vision (ru'yah).
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a combined statement of doctrine and proof for the beatific vision (ru'yat Allah). It begins by affirming that believers will see Allah in Paradise with their physical eyes, but immediately qualifies this: 'without likeness (bila tashbih), without modality (bila kayfiyya), without quantity (bila kamiyya), and there is no distance between Him and His creation.' This is the standard Sunni position that the vision is real but utterly unlike any creaturely vision. The author then supports this with the well-known hadith reported by twenty-one Companions: 'You will not be crowded in seeing Him' (or 'you will not be harmed'), which is in the Sahihs. The longer hadith from Muslim describes the joy of looking at Allah's Face, and the author clarifies that the 'increase' (ziyada) in Surah Yunus 26 refers to this vision. The commentary then stresses that the vision is 'accompanied by transcendence (tanzih) not enclosed in likeness,' rejecting any modality, quantity, direction, connection, separation, indwelling, or union, which are creeds of the existentialists (wujudiyya). The key principle: we affirm what revelation establishes and negate what reason demands transcendence from, citing the verse 'Eyesight does not comprehend Him' (al-An'am 103) to show that comprehension (idrak) is more specific than vision (ru'yah). Thus the vision is affirmed without the limitations of creaturely sight.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Affirming beatific vision implies anthropomorphism or that Allah is like created beings.
Correction: The passage explicitly negates all creaturely likeness (tashbih), modality (kayfiyya), quantity (kamiyya), distance, and other limitations, maintaining that the vision is real but completely transcendent.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Sunni creed affirms the vision of Allah in Paradise but absolutely denies any likeness, modality, or limitation, balancing scriptural affirmation with rational transcendence.

?
The scholars affirm the vision but negate all modality and likeness. Are you comfortable with not knowing how you will see Him? Can you submit to the mystery without demanding details? In what area of your life are you trying to force Allah into your categories?

Ambiguity in Description Does Not Diminish Certainty
Principle and quotation on ambiguous attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how ambiguous descriptions of divine attributes do not undermine the certainty of core truths established by revelation.

Core Idea
Ambiguity in how something happens does not cast doubt on the fact that it happens.

The Qur'an contains verses that describe Allah's attributes in ways that are clear in their affirmation of the reality but unclear in the modality. Some might think this uncertainty makes the belief itself uncertain. In this passage, the author explains that such ambiguity regarding descriptions that reason cannot accommodate does not harm our knowledge of the original truth that aligns with revelation. Imam Abu Hanifa then summarizes the creed for the vision of Allah in the afterlife.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with care
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the flow. Notice that there are two parts: a principle and a quotation.
2
Understand the principle
The author states that ambiguity in a description that reason prevents does not impair knowledge of the original that conforms to transmission. Identify what 'description that reason prevents' means — any creaturely modality.
This is the key tool for handling mutashabih attributes.
3
Examine the quotation from Imam Abu Hanifa
Read his statement about the meeting with Allah in Paradise. Notice the affirmation (haqq) and the negations (without how, likeness, direction). This is a model for affirming core truth while denying modality.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the wrong belief that ambiguity makes the belief uncertain. The passage shows that the core truth remains certain because it is established by clear revelation; only the modality is ambiguous.
This is a crucial principle in Sunni theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتشابه فيما يرجع إلى الوصف الذي يمنعه العقل لا يقدح في العلم بالأصل المطابق للنقل. وقال الإمام الأعظم رحمه الله في كتابه "الوصية": ولقاء الله تعالى لأهل الجنة بلا كيف ولا تشبيه ولا جهة حق انتهى.
And ambiguity with regard to the description that reason prevents does not impair knowledge of the original that conforms to the transmitted proof. And Imam al-A'zam (may Allah have mercy on him) said in his book al-Wasiyya: 'The meeting of Allah Most High for the people of Paradise is without how, without likeness, and without direction — it is true.' End quote.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory principle that addresses a potential objection: if the verse about vision is ambiguous (mutashabih) regarding direction, quantity, and modality, how can we be certain of the vision itself? The author states that such ambiguity in the description that reason prevents (i.e., any creaturely modality) does not impair (la yadqah) the knowledge of the original (al-asl) that conforms to the transmitted proof (al-naql). In other words, the core truth that believers will see Allah is clearly established by revelation; the difficulty is only in how that occurs, and here reason says 'not like this' and we accept that. He then quotes Imam Abu Hanifa's famous statement from his work al-Wasiyya: 'The meeting (liqa') of Allah for the people of Paradise is without how, without likeness, and without direction — it is true (haqq).' This succinctly summarizes the creed: affirmation of the reality (haqq) with negation of modality, anthropomorphism, and spatial direction.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if a verse is ambiguous or cannot be understood fully, then the belief it conveys is uncertain or doubtful.
Correction: The passage clarifies that ambiguity applies only to the description that reason prevents, not to the core reality. The core reality remains certainly known because it is clearly established by revelation and rational evidence.

Key Takeaway
Even when the modality of a divine attribute is ambiguous, the core reality affirmed by revelation remains certain.

Vision of Allah is Additional to Knowledge
Proves that vision is superadded to knowledge using analogy and scripture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the vision of Allah is considered an additional perfection beyond mere knowledge, and how proofs from reason and revelation support this.

Core Idea
Vision is not just knowledge; it is a higher, complete unveiling.

The author now addresses a crucial distinction: vision (ru'yah) is not merely an intensification of knowledge but an entirely different mode of perception. He uses the simple analogy of looking at the moon to show that direct sight gives a completeness that intellectual knowledge alone does not. This lesson unpacks that argument and its scriptural supports.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English to get the overall argument.
2
Understand the moon analogy
Focus on the moon example: why seeing the moon with open eyes is more perfect than knowing it with closed eyes.
This illustrates the difference between 'knowledge' and 'vision'.
3
Examine the scriptural testimonies
Notice the hadith and Quranic verses cited (Prophet's statement, Abraham, Moses). They all point to a higher rank than mere knowledge.
4
Grasp the conclusion about miraculous vision
Understand that Allah's vision is not limited by physical facing; it is a special relation.
The Prophet seeing behind his back is a parallel.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعنى أنه يحصل النظر بأن ينكشف انكشافا تاما بالبصر منزها المقابلة والجهة والهيئة فهي أمر زائد على صفة العلم، فإنا إذا نظرنا إلى البدر مثلا بعين البصر ثم غمضنا العين عن النظر فلا خفاء في أنه وإن كان منكشفا لدينا في الحالين لكن انكشافه حال النظر إليه أتم وأكمل، وهذا معنى قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: ((ليس الخبر كالمعاينة)). وقول إبراهيم عليه السلام {وَلَكِنْ لِيَطْمَئِنَّ قَلْبِي} [البقرة: 260]، فإن عين اليقين رتبة فوق علم اليقين، ومن هنا قال موسى عليه السلام: {رَبِّ أَرِنِي أَنظُرْ إِلَيْكَ} [الأعراف: 143]. والحاصل أن رؤيته تكون على وجه خارق للعادة من غير اعتبار المقابلة لهذه الحاسة، كما روي عنه صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم: ((أتموا صفوفكم فإني أراكم من وراء ظهري)). وكما يرانا الله تعالى اتفاقا، فإن الرؤية نسبة خاصة بين طرفي الرائي والمرئي ومتعلقي رؤيتهما.
And the meaning is that vision (al-nazar) occurs by a complete unveiling through the sight, free from facing, direction, and form; so it is something additional to the attribute of knowledge. For if we look at the moon, for example, with the eye of sight, and then we close the eye from looking, there is no concealment that although it is unveiled to us in both states, its unveiling at the time of looking is more perfect and complete. And this is the meaning of his saying, peace and blessings be upon him: 'The report is not like the eyewitness.' And the saying of Abraham, peace be upon him: {But that my heart may be reassured} [al-Baqarah: 260]; for the eye of certainty is a rank above the knowledge of certainty. And from this, Moses, peace be upon him, said: {My Lord, show me that I may look at You} [al-A'raf: 143]. The conclusion is that His vision occurs in a miraculous manner, without consideration of facing for this sense, as it is related from him, peace and blessings of Allah be upon him: 'Complete your rows, for I see you from behind my back.' And just as Allah sees us, by agreement; for vision is a specific relation between the two sides of the seer and the seen, and the two objects of their seeing.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the vision of Allah (ru'yah) is an additional perfection beyond knowledge ('ilm). The author argues that vision involves a complete unveiling (inkishaf tamm) through the physical sight, but purified of facing, direction, and form. It is therefore something extra (za'id) over the attribute of knowledge. To illustrate, he uses the example of seeing the moon: when you look at the moon, it is unveiled to you; when you close your eyes, you still have knowledge of it, but the unveiling during sight is more complete. This is the meaning of the hadith 'The report is not like the eyewitness' — knowledge from report is less perfect than direct vision. He then cites Prophet Abraham's request for reassurance (Surah al-Baqarah 260) to show that even after knowledge, a higher rank (ain al-yaqin) exists. Prophet Moses' request to see Allah (Surah al-A'raf 143) also demonstrates that vision is a higher level. The conclusion (al-hasil) is that the vision of Allah is miraculous (khariq li'l-ada), not requiring facing, as the Prophet could see behind his back. And just as Allah sees us without facing (by consensus), so vision is a special relation (nisba khassa) between seer and seen, not necessarily requiring physical confrontation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Vision is just more knowledge or a metaphor for understanding.
Correction: The passage argues that vision is a distinct, more perfect unveiling beyond knowledge, as shown by the analogy and scriptural evidence.

Key Takeaway

Linguistic Analysis of Nazar (Seeing Allah)
The author enumerates three Quranic usages of nazar to prove it means physical sight when used with ila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic reasons why the word 'nazar' in the Quranic verse about seeing Allah refers to physical vision.

Core Idea
The Arabic word 'nazar' takes its meaning from its particle; with 'ila' it always means physical sight.

In this passage, the author unpacks the Arabic word 'nazar' to show that its usage in the verse about the believers seeing Allah on the Day of Judgment is clear evidence for physical vision. By examining how the Quran itself uses 'nazar' with different particles, the author demonstrates that the verse's construction leaves no room for metaphorical interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the key claim
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice that the author argues that the verse explicitly refers to physical sight based on linguistic features.
Pay attention to the words 'wajh' (face) and 'ila'.
2
Identify the three usages of nazar
The author lists three Quranic usages of 'nazar' depending on the particle. Find each one and understand the meaning associated with each particle.
The three particles: no particle (by itself), 'fi' (in), and 'ila' (to).
3
Apply to the verse about seeing Allah
Notice that the verse uses 'nazar' with 'ila' and attributes it to the face, which is the locus of eyesight. This combination confirms physical vision.
4
Consider the Hasan al-Basri quote
The quote from Hasan al-Basri supports the interpretation that the faces look at the Lord by His light, implying vision without modality.
This quote does not negate physical sight but explains how it occurs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن إضافة النظر إلى الوجه الذي هو محله في هذه الآية وتعديته بإلى الصريحة في نظر العين وإخلاء الكلام من قرينة تدل على خلاف حقيقته، وموضوعه صريح في أنه الله تعالى أراد بذلك نظر العين التي في الوجه إلى الرب جل جلاله، فإن النظر له عدة استعمالات بحسب صلاته واختلاف متعلقاته وتعديته بنفسه، فإنه إن عدي بنفسه فمعناه التوقيف والانتظار كقوله تعالى: انْظُرُونَا تَقْتَبِسُ مِنْ نُورِكُمْ [الحديد: 13]، وقوله تعالى: لَا تَقُولُوا رَاعِنَا وَقُولُو انْظُرْنَا [البقرة: 104]، وإن عدي بفي فمعناه التفكر والاعتبار كقوله تعالى: ﴿أَوَلَمْ يَنْظُرُوا فِي مَلَكُوتِ السَّمواتِ والأرض﴾ [الأعراف: 185]، وإن عدي بإلى فمعناه المعاينة بالأبصار كقوله تعالى: وانْظُرُوا إلى ثَمَرِةٍ إِذَا أَثْمَرَ [الأنعام: 99]، فكيف إذا أضيف إلى الوجه الذي هو محل البصر. قال الحسن البصري: نظرت أي الوجوه إلى ربها فنظرت بنوره ولا يلزم من الرؤية الإدراك والإحاطة، فلا ينافي قوله تعالى: لا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ [الأنعام: 103]، فإن الإدراك هو الإحاطة بالشيء، وهو قدر زائد على الرؤية كما قال الله تعالى: فَلَمَّا تَرَاءَ الْجَمْعَانِ قَالَ أَصْحَبُ مُوسَى إِنَّا لَمُدْرَكُونَ قَالَ كَلَّا [الشعراء: 61-62]، فلم ينف موسى الرؤية وإنما نفى الإدراك، فالرب تعالى يرى ولا يدرك كما يعلم ولا يحاط به علما، بل هذه الشمس المخلوقة لا يتمكن رائيها من إدراكها على ما هي
Then know that the attribution of 'nazar' to the face, which is its locus in this verse, and its transitivity with 'ila' which is explicit for the sight of the eye, and the absence of any contextual evidence indicating otherwise, and its subject matter is explicit that Allah the Exalted intended thereby the sight of the eye that is in the face toward the Lord, Mighty and Majestic. For 'nazar' has several usages according to its particle and different objects and its transitivity by itself: when it is made transitive by itself, its meaning is tawqif (making pause) and intidhar (waiting), as in His saying: 'Look at us (unzuruna) that we may borrow from your light' (Quran 57:13), and His saying: 'Do not say ra'ina but say unzurna' (Quran 2:104); and when it is made transitive with 'fi', its meaning is tafakkur (reflection) and i'tibar (taking heed), as in His saying: 'Do they not look (yanzuru) into the dominion of the heavens and the earth' (Quran 7:185); and when it is made transitive with 'ila', its meaning is mu'ayana (seeing) with the eyes, as in His saying: 'And look at (unzuru ila) the fruit when it yields fruit' (Quran 6:99). So how about when it is attributed to the face, which is the locus of sight? Al-Hasan al-Basri said: 'The faces looked at their Lord and looked by His light.' And vision does not necessitate perception (idrak) and encompassing (ihata), so it does not contradict His saying: 'Vision perceives Him not' (Quran 6:103). For perception is encompassing of a thing, and it is an additional degree beyond vision, as Allah said: 'When the two hosts saw each other, the companions of Moses said: Indeed we are overtaken (mudrakun). He said: No' (Quran 26:61-62). So Moses did not deny the vision but rather denied perception. Thus the Lord, Exalted, is seen but not perceived, just as He is known but not encompassed in knowledge. Indeed, even this created sun, its seer is not able to perceive it as it is.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a detailed linguistic and theological argument about the word 'nazar' in the Quranic verse that promises the believers a vision of Allah (Quran 75:22-23). The author argues that the verse is explicit in referring to physical sight of the eyes. He supports this by analyzing the different uses of 'nazar' in the Quran. First, he notes that the word is attached to 'face' (wajh) and made transitive with 'ila', which is the particle used for physical sight. There is no contextual evidence to suggest a metaphorical meaning. Then he enumerates the three common usages of 'nazar' in Arabic: (1) when transitive by itself (without a particle), it means 'waiting' or 'pausing' (e.g., Quran 57:13 and 2:104); (2) when transitive with 'fi', it means 'reflecting' (e.g., Quran 7:185); (3) when transitive with 'ila', it means 'seeing with the eyes' (e.g., Quran 6:99). Since the verse about seeing Allah uses 'nazar' with 'ila' and mentions 'faces', it clearly means physical sight. Then the author cites a statement from Hasan al-Basri who interprets 'looking' as 'looking by His light', which does not imply physical modality. The author then addresses the apparent contradiction with Quran 6:103 ('Vision perceives Him not'). He explains that idrak (perception) is an additional concept meaning encompassing (ihata), and it is not the same as mere vision (ru'ya). He cites the story of Moses and the two hosts (Quran 26:61-62) where Moses denied idrak (being overtaken) but did not deny ru'ya (seeing). Therefore, Allah can be seen but not fully perceived or encompassed. The author concludes with an analogy: even the created sun cannot be perceived (idrak) in its full reality by the viewer, though it is seen. Thus, the verse denying idrak does not negate ru'ya.

Key Terms
النظر
إلى

المعاينة

The Difference Between Seeing Allah and Encompassing Him
The author distinguishes between ru'ya and idrak to resolve the apparent contradiction between Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the verse 'Vision perceives Him not' does not contradict the believers' vision of Allah, because idrak (perception) is a form of encompassing beyond ru'ya (vision).

Core Idea
Seeing Allah does not mean fully encompassing Him; idrak is more than ru'ya.

After establishing that the verse about seeing Allah refers to physical vision, the author addresses a potential objection: Quran 6:103 states 'Vision perceives Him not.' He clarifies that idrak (perception) is not the same as ru'ya (vision); idrak implies complete encompassing, which is impossible for Allah. He uses the story of Moses and the Egyptian army to illustrate that one can see something without perceiving it fully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the key distinction
Read the Arabic and translation. Pay attention to the terms 'ru'ya' (vision) and 'idrak' (perception). The author argues that idrak is an additional concept meaning encompassing (ihata).
2
Understand the proof from the Moses story
The author cites Quran 26:61-62 where the companions of Moses say 'Inna la mudrakun' (we are overtaken). Moses replies 'Kalla' (no), denying idrak but not ru'ya. This shows that idrak is not the same as vision.
The key is that mudrakun here means 'overtaken' or 'perceived/captured', not 'seen'.
3
Apply the analogy of the sun
The author uses the example of the created sun: one can see it but cannot fully perceive (idrak) its true reality. Similarly, Allah is seen but cannot be encompassed.
This analogy helps reconcile the verses.
4
Reflect on the theological implications
The distinction preserves divine transcendence: Allah can be seen yet remains beyond full comprehension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن إضافة النظر إلى الوجه الذي هو محله في هذه الآية وتعديته بإلى الصريحة في نظر العين وإخلاء الكلام من قرينة تدل على خلاف حقيقته، وموضوعه صريح في أنه الله تعالى أراد بذلك نظر العين التي في الوجه إلى الرب جل جلاله، فإن النظر له عدة استعمالات بحسب صلاته واختلاف متعلقاته وتعديته بنفسه، فإنه إن عدي بنفسه فمعناه التوقيف والانتظار كقوله تعالى: انْظُرُونَا تَقْتَبِسُ مِنْ نُورِكُمْ [الحديد: 13]، وقوله تعالى: لَا تَقُولُوا رَاعِنَا وَقُولُو انْظُرْنَا [البقرة: 104]، وإن عدي بفي فمعناه التفكر والاعتبار كقوله تعالى: ﴿أَوَلَمْ يَنْظُرُوا فِي مَلَكُوتِ السَّمواتِ والأرض﴾ [الأعراف: 185]، وإن عدي بإلى فمعناه المعاينة بالأبصار كقوله تعالى: وانْظُرُوا إلى ثَمَرِةٍ إِذَا أَثْمَرَ [الأنعام: 99]، فكيف إذا أضيف إلى الوجه الذي هو محل البصر. قال الحسن البصري: نظرت أي الوجوه إلى ربها فنظرت بنوره ولا يلزم من الرؤية الإدراك والإحاطة، فلا ينافي قوله تعالى: لا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ [الأنعام: 103]، فإن الإدراك هو الإحاطة بالشيء، وهو قدر زائد على الرؤية كما قال الله تعالى: فَلَمَّا تَرَاءَ الْجَمْعَانِ قَالَ أَصْحَبُ مُوسَى إِنَّا لَمُدْرَكُونَ قَالَ كَلَّا [الشعراء: 61-62]، فلم ينف موسى الرؤية وإنما نفى الإدراك، فالرب تعالى يرى ولا يدرك كما يعلم ولا يحاط به علما، بل هذه الشمس المخلوقة لا يتمكن رائيها من إدراكها على ما هي
Then know that the attribution of 'nazar' to the face, which is its locus in this verse, and its transitivity with 'ila' which is explicit for the sight of the eye, and the absence of any contextual evidence indicating otherwise, and its subject matter is explicit that Allah the Exalted intended thereby the sight of the eye that is in the face toward the Lord, Mighty and Majestic. For 'nazar' has several usages according to its particle and different objects and its transitivity by itself: when it is made transitive by itself, its meaning is tawqif (making pause) and intidhar (waiting), as in His saying: 'Look at us (unzuruna) that we may borrow from your light' (Quran 57:13), and His saying: 'Do not say ra'ina but say unzurna' (Quran 2:104); and when it is made transitive with 'fi', its meaning is tafakkur (reflection) and i'tibar (taking heed), as in His saying: 'Do they not look (yanzuru) into the dominion of the heavens and the earth' (Quran 7:185); and when it is made transitive with 'ila', its meaning is mu'ayana (seeing) with the eyes, as in His saying: 'And look at (unzuru ila) the fruit when it yields fruit' (Quran 6:99). So how about when it is attributed to the face, which is the locus of sight? Al-Hasan al-Basri said: 'The faces looked at their Lord and looked by His light.' And vision does not necessitate perception (idrak) and encompassing (ihata), so it does not contradict His saying: 'Vision perceives Him not' (Quran 6:103). For perception is encompassing of a thing, and it is an additional degree beyond vision, as Allah said: 'When the two hosts saw each other, the companions of Moses said: Indeed we are overtaken (mudrakun). He said: No' (Quran 26:61-62). So Moses did not deny the vision but rather denied perception. Thus the Lord, Exalted, is seen but not perceived, just as He is known but not encompassed in knowledge. Indeed, even this created sun, its seer is not able to perceive it as it is.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a detailed linguistic and theological argument about the word 'nazar' in the Quranic verse that promises the believers a vision of Allah (Quran 75:22-23). The author argues that the verse is explicit in referring to physical sight of the eyes. He supports this by analyzing the different uses of 'nazar' in the Quran. First, he notes that the word is attached to 'face' (wajh) and made transitive with 'ila', which is the particle used for physical sight. There is no contextual evidence to suggest a metaphorical meaning. Then he enumerates the three common usages of 'nazar' in Arabic: (1) when transitive by itself (without a particle), it means 'waiting' or 'pausing' (e.g., Quran 57:13 and 2:104); (2) when transitive with 'fi', it means 'reflecting' (e.g., Quran 7:185); (3) when transitive with 'ila', it means 'seeing with the eyes' (e.g., Quran 6:99). Since the verse about seeing Allah uses 'nazar' with 'ila' and mentions 'faces', it clearly means physical sight. Then the author cites a statement from Hasan al-Basri who interprets 'looking' as 'looking by His light', which does not imply physical modality. The author then addresses the apparent contradiction with Quran 6:103 ('Vision perceives Him not'). He explains that idrak (perception) is an additional concept meaning encompassing (ihata), and it is not the same as mere vision (ru'ya). He cites the story of Moses and the two hosts (Quran 26:61-62) where Moses denied idrak (being overtaken) but did not deny ru'ya (seeing). Therefore, Allah can be seen but not fully perceived or encompassed. The author concludes with an analogy: even the created sun cannot be perceived (idrak) in its full reality by the viewer, though it is seen. Thus, the verse denying idrak does not negate ru'ya.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'idrak' is synonymous with 'ru'ya', so the verse 'Vision perceives Him not' negates the possibility of seeing Allah.
Correction: The passage explains that idrak is a form of complete encompassing, not mere vision. Ru'ya is affirmed while idrak is negated.

D) Neither vision nor perception

Key Takeaway
Allah can be seen by the believers in the afterlife, but He cannot be fully encompassed or perceived in His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'La tudrikuhu al-absar', do you feel a sense of awe or do you skim over it? This verse sets a limit on your ability to fully grasp Allah. Are you comfortable with a God you cannot fully comprehend, or does your faith demand control and complete understanding? How does this truth affect your worship and your trust in His commands that go beyond your reason?
?
You will see Allah in Jannah, but you will never fully encompass Him. Does that thought fill you with longing or with frustration? Reflect on the created sun that you cannot even fully perceive. How does this humble your intellectual pride and remind you of your place before your Lord?
?
The author argues that 'idrak' is more than 'ru'ya'. Have you ever confused the two, thinking that seeing something means fully understanding it? How does this distinction challenge your assumptions about your relationship with Allah? Are there aspects of your faith where you seek to 'encompass' Allah with your mind rather than submit to His majesty?

The Non-Existent Is Not a Thing
Argument that al-ma'dum is not a thing with Quranic reconciliation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to Sunni theology, the non-existent (al-ma'dum) is not a 'thing' (shay'), and how the Quranic verses are reconciled.

Core Idea
The non-existent is not a thing; even the Quran's calling something a thing before existence refers to its future outcome.

This lesson tackles a key theological distinction: whether something that does not yet exist can be called a 'thing'. The author presents a Quranic challenge and then clarifies the Sunni position, showing how apparent contradictions are resolved through the principle of considering the outcome (al-ma'al).

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
Consider why someone might think the non-existent can be called a thing. The Qur'an itself seems to call the earthquake of the Hour a 'tremendous thing' before it happens.
2
Examine the counter-evidence
Read Surah al-Hajj verse 1: 'Indeed, the earthquake of the Hour is a tremendous thing.' This verse appears to call something non-existent a 'thing'.
This is the apparent proof for the opposing view.
3
The Sunni proof from Surah Maryam
Read Surah Maryam verse 9: 'And I have created you before, when you were not a thing.' This explicitly denies that something before existence is a thing. The author insists this verse cannot be interpreted away.
This is the decisive proof for the Sunni position.
4
Reconcile the verses
Understand that the verse from al-Hajj uses 'thing' in light of the future outcome (al-ma'al). The earthquake is called a thing because it will be a tremendous event when it occurs, not because it is a thing in its non-existent state.
This resolution maintains consistency: the non-existent is not a thing, but can be described by its future reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لاستحالة إيجاد الموجود، فتعين أن يكون المراد منه المعدوم ولقوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ زَلْزَلَةَ السَّاعَةِ شَيْءٍ عَظِيمٌ ﴾ [الحج: ١]، سمى الزلزلة قبل وجودها شيئا. وعندنا المعدوم ليس بشيء لقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَقَدْ خَلَقْتُكَ مِنْ قَبْلُ وَلَمْ تَكُ شَيْان [مريم: 9]، فالله تعالى أخبر أنه لم يكن شيئا قبل الوجود، وهذا لا يحتمل التأويل، فكيف يكون المعدوم شيئا؟ فتسمية الشيء في الآيتين السابقتين باعتبار المآل والله أعلم بالحال، وسيأتي زيادة تحقيق لذلك.
Because of the impossibility of bringing the existent into existence, it is determined that what is intended by it is the non-existent. And by His saying: 'Indeed, the earthquake of the Hour is a tremendous thing' (al-Hajj 1), He called the earthquake before its existence a thing. According to us, the non-existent is not a thing, because of His saying: 'And I have created you before, when you were not a thing' (Maryam 9). So Allah Almighty informed that he was not a thing before existence, and this does not admit interpretation. So how can the non-existent be a thing? Thus the naming of 'thing' in the two preceding verses is with consideration of the outcome (al-ma'al), and Allah knows best the state of affairs. And there will come a further verification of that.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is an argument about whether the non-existent (al-ma'dum) can be called a 'thing' (shay'). The author begins by referencing a previous argument that it is impossible to bring the existent into existence again; therefore the intended meaning must be the non-existent. He then cites the Quranic verse calling the earthquake of the Hour a 'tremendous thing' before it exists, which might suggest the non-existent is a thing. But he immediately clarifies that according to the Sunni position (عندنا), the non-existent is not a thing. He provides the proof from Surah Maryam, where Allah says He created man when he was 'not a thing,' explicitly denying that something before existence is a thing. The author insists this verse does not admit interpretation (لا يحتمل التأويل), so the non-existent cannot be a thing. He then explains the apparent contradiction: the earlier verse from al-Hajj uses 'thing' of the earthquake in terms of its future outcome (باعتبار المآل). The author concludes by saying that further verification will follow.

Key Terms
المعدوم (al-ma'dum)
الشيء (al-shay')
المآل (al-ma'al)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quran calling the earthquake a 'thing' before it exists proves that the non-existent is a thing.
Correction: The author clarifies that this verse uses 'thing' with consideration of the future outcome (al-ma'al), not as a description of the current non-existent state.

Checkpoint

B) Surah Maryam verse 9
C) Surah al-An'am verse 103
D) Surah al-Baqarah verse 104

Key Takeaway
The non-existent (al-ma'dum) cannot be called a 'thing'; any Quranic use of 'thing' for something before its existence refers to its future state (al-ma'al).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that the non-existent is not a thing. Think of a future event you are anxious about—an exam, a conversation, or an uncertainty. In your mind, are you treating that future possibility as if it already exists? How does knowing it is not a thing—right now—free you from unnecessary worry? Will you choose to remind yourself of this truth the next time anxiety strikes?
?
Reflect on the past: Are there mistakes or missed opportunities you still dwell on? In light of this lesson, those moments are non-existent now—they are not 'things' in your present reality. How might this perspective help you let go of past regrets and focus on what actually exists in your life today? Will you actively practice releasing that mental grip?
?
The verse from Maryam tells you that before existence, you were not a thing. Does this humble you? Do you live as if your existence and accomplishments are entirely from Allah, or do you sometimes feel entitled or self-sufficient? How can you make this truth a living reality in your daily gratitude and reliance on Him?

Linguistic Uses of Nazar and Literal Sight
The author analyzes the linguistic uses of 'nazar' to prove that 'ru'ya' in the verse is literal sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the linguistic analysis of the word 'nazar' in the Quranic verse demonstrates that it refers to literal sight of Allah in the Hereafter.

Core Idea
The word 'nazar' means physical sight when followed by 'ila' and attributed to the eye.

This lesson dives into the linguistic reasoning of the author to prove that the verse 'Wujuhun yawma'idhin nadirah... ila rabbiha nazirah' actually means that believers will see Allah with their eyes. The author carefully examines the word 'nazar' and its various uses in Arabic, showing that the specific grammatical construction here forces the literal meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's argument.
Notice the three key clues the author identifies in the verse.
2
Identify the three evidences from the verse
The author points out that (1) 'nazar' is attributed to the face, (2) it is transitive with 'ila', and (3) there is no contextual indicator for a metaphorical meaning. List these in your own words.
3
Learn the three categories of 'nazar'
The author classifies 'nazar' based on its transitivity: by itself (waiting), with 'fi' (reflection), with 'ila' (sight). Memorise these with Quranic examples.
This classification is key to understanding the argument.
4
Apply the categories to the verse
Since the verse uses 'ila' and mentions the face, the only consistent meaning is literal sight. See how the author concludes that Allah intended physical vision.
This step connects the linguistic analysis to the theological conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن إضافة النظر إلى الوجه الذي هو محله في هذه الآية وتعديته بإلى الصريحة في نظر العين وإخلاء الكلام من قرينة تدل على خلاف حقيقته، وموضوعه صريح في أنه الله تعالى أراد بذلك نظر العين التي في الوجه إلى الرب جل جلاله، فإن النظر له عدة استعمالات بحسب صلاته واختلاف متعلقاته وتعديته بنفسه، فإنه إن عدي بنفسه فمعناه التوقيف والانتظار كقوله تعالى: انْظُرُونَا تَقْتَبِسُ مِنْ نُورِكُمْ [الحديد: ۱۳]، وقوله تعالى: لَا تَقُولُوا رَاعِنَا وَقُولُو انْظُرْنَا [البقرة: ١٠٤]، وإن عدي بفي فمعناه التفكر والاعتبار كقوله تعالى: ﴿ أَوَلَمْ يَنْظُرُوا فِي مَلَكُوتِ السَّمواتِ والأرض [الأعراف: ١٨٥]، وإن عدي بإلى فمعناه المعاينة بالأبصار كقوله تعالى : وانْظُرُوا إلى ثَمَرِةٍ إِذَا أَثْمَرَ﴾ [الأنعام: ۹۹] ، فكيف إذا أضيف إلى الوجه الذي هو محل البصر. قال الحسن البصري: نظرت أي الوجوه إلى ربها فنظرت بنوره ولا يلزم من الرؤية الإدراك والإحاطة، فلا ينافي قوله تعالى : لا تُدْرِ كُهُ الْاَبْصَارُ ﴾ [الأنعام: ۱۰۳] ، فإن الإدراك هو الإحاطة بالشيء، وهو قدر زائد على الرؤية كما قال الله تعالى: فَلَمَّا تَرَ آءَ الْجَمْعَنِ قَالَ أَصْحَبُ مُوسَى إِنَّا لَمُدْرَكُونَ قَالَ كَلَّا [الشعراء: ٦١-٦٢] ، فلم ينف موسى الرؤية وإنما نفى الإدراك، فالرب تعالى يرى ولا يدرك كما يعلم ولا يحاط به علما، بل هذه الشمس المخلوقة لا يتمكن رائيها من إدراكها على ما هي عليه من حقيقة ذاتها، وقد تواترت أحاديث إثبات الرؤية تواتراً معنويا فيجب قبولها نقلا ولا يلتفت إلى ما يتوهمه أهل البدعة عقلا ولقد أخطأ شارح عقيدة الطحاوي في هذه المسألة حيث قال: فهل يعقل رؤية بلا مقابلة؟ وفيه دليل على علوه على خلقه. انتهى. وكأنه قائل بالجهة العلوية لربه، ومذهب أهل السنة والجماعة أنه سبحانه لا يرى في جهة، وقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: سترون) ربكم كما ترون القمر ليلة (البدر)) . ) تشبيه للرؤية بالرؤية في الجملة، لا تشبيه المرئي بالمرئي من جميع الوجوه.
Then know that the attribution of 'nazar' to the face, which is its locus in this verse, and its transitivity with 'ila' explicit in the sight of the eye, and the absence of any contextual indicator suggesting contrary to its literal meaning, and its subject is explicit that Allah Almighty intended by that the sight of the eye which is in the face towards the Lord, the Exalted is His Majesty. For 'nazar' has several uses depending on its connectors and the difference of its objects and its transitivity by itself. If it is made transitive by itself, its meaning is waiting and expectation, as in His saying: 'Wait for us; we will borrow from your light' (al-Hadid 13), and His saying: 'Do not say ra'ina but say unzurna' (al-Baqarah 104). If it is made transitive with 'fi', its meaning is reflection and consideration, as in His saying: 'Have they not looked in the dominion of the heavens and the earth' (al-A'raf 185). If it is made transitive with 'ila', its meaning is seeing with the eyes, as in His saying: 'And look at its fruit when it bears fruit' (al-An'am 99). So how about when it is attributed to the face, which is the locus of sight? Al-Hasan al-Basri said: The faces looked at their Lord and saw by His light. And sight does not necessarily entail perception and encompassment, so it does not contradict His saying: 'Sight does not perceive Him' (al-An'am 103). For perception is encompassment of a thing, and it is an additional quality beyond sight, as Allah said: 'When the two companies saw each other, the companions of Moses said: Indeed we are overtaken. He said: No' (al-Shu'ara 61-62). So Moses did not deny sight, but only denied perception. So the Lord, Exalted, is seen but not perceived, just as He is known but not encompassed in knowledge. Rather, even this created sun, its viewer cannot perceive it as it really is. And the hadiths affirming ru'ya have been transmitted with a tawatur ma'nawi (mass transmission in meaning), so they must be accepted by transmission, and no attention is paid to what the people of innovation imagine by reason. And indeed the commentator of 'Aqidat al-Tahawi made a mistake in this issue when he said: 'Is a vision without facing rationally conceivable? And in it there is evidence for His being above His creation.' End. As if he were asserting a direction of above for his Lord. And the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a is that He, glorified, is not seen in a direction. And his saying, peace and blessings be upon him: 'You will see your Lord as you see the moon on the night of the full moon' is a comparison of vision to vision in general, not a comparison of the seen to the seen in all respects.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is both a linguistic argument and a theological defense of the possibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya) in the Hereafter. The author begins by examining the word 'nazar' (look) in a specific Quranic verse. He notes three points: (1) the word is attributed to the face, (2) it is made transitive with 'ila' (to), and (3) there is no contextual indicator that it means anything other than literal seeing. Therefore, Allah intended the literal sight of the eye directed towards the Lord. Then the author classifies the uses of nazar based on its transitivity: by itself it means waiting; with 'fi' it means reflection; with 'ila' it means seeing with the eyes. He quotes Quranic examples for each. Since the verse under discussion uses 'ila' and mentions the face, it clearly means physical vision. He then cites Hasan al-Basri affirming that the faces look at their Lord. Anticipating an objection that seeing implies perception (idrak) and encompassment, which the Quran denies in 'La tudrikuhu al-absar', the author clarifies that ru'ya (sight) is distinct from idrak (perception). Idrak is complete encompassment. He proves this with the story of Moses and the magicians: when the two companies saw each other, the people of Moses said 'inna lamudrakun' (we are overtaken), but Moses denied only idrak, not ru'ya. So Allah can be seen but not fully perceived, just as He is known but not completely encompassed. He gives the example of the sun: we see it but cannot perceive its complete reality. Next, he invokes the mutawatir hadiths about ru'ya as a decisive transmitted proof, overriding rational objections from heretics. He then criticizes the commentator of 'Aqidat al-Tahawi who argued that vision requires facing (muqabala) and implies direction (jiha). The author states that the Sunni position is that Allah is not seen in a direction. Finally, he interprets the hadith comparing seeing Allah to seeing the full moon as a comparison of vision to vision, not of the seen to the seen, thus avoiding anthropomorphism.

Key Terms
النظر (al-nazar)
الرؤية (al-ru'ya)
المعاينة (al-mu'ayana)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'nazar' in the verse means 'waiting' or 'reflection'.
Correction: The author shows that when 'nazar' is made transitive with 'ila' and attributed to the face, it must mean physical sight, not waiting or reflection.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the meaning of 'nazar' when made transitive with 'ila' (إلى)?
A) Waiting and expectation
B) Reflection and consideration
C) Seeing with the eyes
D) Knowledge and certitude

Key Takeaway
The linguistic construction of the verse forces the meaning of literal sight of Allah in the Hereafter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If you were to argue for the literal sight of Allah in the Hereafter, would you be able to present the linguistic evidence from the Quran as the author does? How will you commit to learning the Arabic of the Quran to defend the correct understanding of Allah's attributes?
?
Imagine standing before Allah on the Day of Judgment, and He asks you: 'Did you believe in My words as revealed, or did you twist them to fit your own reasoning?' What will your answer be based on your study of this verse?

Ru'ya vs Idrak and No Direction
The author distinguishes ru'ya from idrak and refutes the notion of direction in ru'ya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between ru'ya (seeing) and idrak (comprehensive perception), and why the hadiths about ru'ya do not imply direction for Allah.

Core Idea
Seeing Allah does not mean fully perceiving Him, and ru'ya occurs without direction.

After establishing that the verse commands literal sight of Allah, the author addresses two potential objections: that sight implies full perception (which the Quran denies) and that sight requires a direction (which would limit Allah). He clarifies the difference between ru'ya and idrak, and refutes the notion of direction. He also proves ru'ya from hadith and correctly interprets the comparison to the moon.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read from 'ولا يلزم من الرؤية الإدراك' to the end. Notice how the author anticipates and responds to objections.
This part builds on the conclusion of the first lesson.
2
Understand the ru'ya-idrak distinction
The author argues that ru'ya (sight) does not entail idrak (full perception). He proves this using the story of Moses in Surah al-Shu'ara'. Identify how Moses denied idrak but not ru'ya.
This distinction is crucial for refuting the objection based on the verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar'.
3
Reflection on the sun analogy
The author gives the example of the created sun: we see it but cannot perceive its complete reality. This illustrates that ru'ya can occur without idrak even in this world.
Think about how this analogy strengthens the argument.
4
Recognise the proof from hadith
The author mentions that hadiths affirming ru'ya are mutawatir in meaning and must be accepted. Note his rejection of rational objections from heretics.
This establishes the transmitted evidence alongside the rational one.
5
Refute the direction objection
The author criticises the commentator of 'Aqidat al-Tahawi for claiming that ru'ya requires direction. Learn the Sunni position: Allah is seen without direction. Also, understand the correct interpretation of the moon hadith: it compares vision to vision, not the seen to the seen.
Pay attention to how the author avoids anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن إضافة النظر إلى الوجه الذي هو محله في هذه الآية وتعديته بإلى الصريحة في نظر العين وإخلاء الكلام من قرينة تدل على خلاف حقيقته، وموضوعه صريح في أنه الله تعالى أراد بذلك نظر العين التي في الوجه إلى الرب جل جلاله، فإن النظر له عدة استعمالات بحسب صلاته واختلاف متعلقاته وتعديته بنفسه، فإنه إن عدي بنفسه فمعناه التوقيف والانتظار كقوله تعالى: انْظُرُونَا تَقْتَبِسُ مِنْ نُورِكُمْ [الحديد: ۱۳]، وقوله تعالى: لَا تَقُولُوا رَاعِنَا وَقُولُو انْظُرْنَا [البقرة: ١٠٤]، وإن عدي بفي فمعناه التفكر والاعتبار كقوله تعالى: ﴿ أَوَلَمْ يَنْظُرُوا فِي مَلَكُوتِ السَّمواتِ والأرض [الأعراف: ١٨٥]، وإن عدي بإلى فمعناه المعاينة بالأبصار كقوله تعالى : وانْظُرُوا إلى ثَمَرِةٍ إِذَا أَثْمَرَ﴾ [الأنعام: ۹۹] ، فكيف إذا أضيف إلى الوجه الذي هو محل البصر. قال الحسن البصري: نظرت أي الوجوه إلى ربها فنظرت بنوره ولا يلزم من الرؤية الإدراك والإحاطة، فلا ينافي قوله تعالى : لا تُدْرِ كُهُ الْاَبْصَارُ ﴾ [الأنعام: ۱۰۳] ، فإن الإدراك هو الإحاطة بالشيء، وهو قدر زائد على الرؤية كما قال الله تعالى: فَلَمَّا تَرَ آءَ الْجَمْعَنِ قَالَ أَصْحَبُ مُوسَى إِنَّا لَمُدْرَكُونَ قَالَ كَلَّا [الشعراء: ٦١-٦٢] ، فلم ينف موسى الرؤية وإنما نفى الإدراك، فالرب تعالى يرى ولا يدرك كما يعلم ولا يحاط به علما، بل هذه الشمس المخلوقة لا يتمكن رائيها من إدراكها على ما هي عليه من حقيقة ذاتها، وقد تواترت أحاديث إثبات الرؤية تواتراً معنويا فيجب قبولها نقلا ولا يلتفت إلى ما يتوهمه أهل البدعة عقلا ولقد أخطأ شارح عقيدة الطحاوي في هذه المسألة حيث قال: فهل يعقل رؤية بلا مقابلة؟ وفيه دليل على علوه على خلقه. انتهى. وكأنه قائل بالجهة العلوية لربه، ومذهب أهل السنة والجماعة أنه سبحانه لا يرى في جهة، وقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: سترون) ربكم كما ترون القمر ليلة (البدر)) . ) تشبيه للرؤية بالرؤية في الجملة، لا تشبيه المرئي بالمرئي من جميع الوجوه.
Then know that the attribution of 'nazar' to the face, which is its locus in this verse, and its transitivity with 'ila' explicit in the sight of the eye, and the absence of any contextual indicator suggesting contrary to its literal meaning, and its subject is explicit that Allah Almighty intended by that the sight of the eye which is in the face towards the Lord, the Exalted is His Majesty. For 'nazar' has several uses depending on its connectors and the difference of its objects and its transitivity by itself. If it is made transitive by itself, its meaning is waiting and expectation, as in His saying: 'Wait for us; we will borrow from your light' (al-Hadid 13), and His saying: 'Do not say ra'ina but say unzurna' (al-Baqarah 104). If it is made transitive with 'fi', its meaning is reflection and consideration, as in His saying: 'Have they not looked in the dominion of the heavens and the earth' (al-A'raf 185). If it is made transitive with 'ila', its meaning is seeing with the eyes, as in His saying: 'And look at its fruit when it bears fruit' (al-An'am 99). So how about when it is attributed to the face, which is the locus of sight? Al-Hasan al-Basri said: The faces looked at their Lord and saw by His light. And sight does not necessarily entail perception and encompassment, so it does not contradict His saying: 'Sight does not perceive Him' (al-An'am 103). For perception is encompassment of a thing, and it is an additional quality beyond sight, as Allah said: 'When the two companies saw each other, the companions of Moses said: Indeed we are overtaken. He said: No' (al-Shu'ara 61-62). So Moses did not deny sight, but only denied perception. So the Lord, Exalted, is seen but not perceived, just as He is known but not encompassed in knowledge. Rather, even this created sun, its viewer cannot perceive it as it really is. And the hadiths affirming ru'ya have been transmitted with a tawatur ma'nawi (mass transmission in meaning), so they must be accepted by transmission, and no attention is paid to what the people of innovation imagine by reason. And indeed the commentator of 'Aqidat al-Tahawi made a mistake in this issue when he said: 'Is a vision without facing rationally conceivable? And in it there is evidence for His being above His creation.' End. As if he were asserting a direction of above for his Lord. And the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a is that He, glorified, is not seen in a direction. And his saying, peace and blessings be upon him: 'You will see your Lord as you see the moon on the night of the full moon' is a comparison of vision to vision in general, not a comparison of the seen to the seen in all respects.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is both a linguistic argument and a theological defense of the possibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya) in the Hereafter. The author begins by examining the word 'nazar' (look) in a specific Quranic verse. He notes three points: (1) the word is attributed to the face, (2) it is made transitive with 'ila' (to), and (3) there is no contextual indicator that it means anything other than literal seeing. Therefore, Allah intended the literal sight of the eye directed towards the Lord. Then the author classifies the uses of nazar based on its transitivity: by itself it means waiting; with 'fi' it means reflection; with 'ila' it means seeing with the eyes. He quotes Quranic examples for each. Since the verse under discussion uses 'ila' and mentions the face, it clearly means physical vision. He then cites Hasan al-Basri affirming that the faces look at their Lord. Anticipating an objection that seeing implies perception (idrak) and encompassment, which the Quran denies in 'La tudrikuhu al-absar', the author clarifies that ru'ya (sight) is distinct from idrak (perception). Idrak is complete encompassment. He proves this with the story of Moses and the magicians: when the two companies saw each other, the people of Moses said 'inna lamudrakun' (we are overtaken), but Moses denied only idrak, not ru'ya. So Allah can be seen but not fully perceived, just as He is known but not completely encompassed. He gives the example of the sun: we see it but cannot perceive its complete reality. Next, he invokes the mutawatir hadiths about ru'ya as a decisive transmitted proof, overriding rational objections from heretics. He then criticizes the commentator of 'Aqidat al-Tahawi who argued that vision requires facing (muqabala) and implies direction (jiha). The author states that the Sunni position is that Allah is not seen in a direction. Finally, he interprets the hadith comparing seeing Allah to seeing the full moon as a comparison of vision to vision, not of the seen to the seen, thus avoiding anthropomorphism.

Key Terms
الرؤية (al-ru'ya)
الإدراك (al-idrak)
الإحاطة (al-ihata)
الجهة (al-jiha)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that seeing Allah (ru'ya) requires a direction (e.g., facing, or above) and that the hadith of the moon implies Allah is like the moon.
Correction: The author states that the Sunni position is that Allah is not seen in any direction, and the hadith compares the act of vision, not the object seen, to the moon.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the relationship between ru'ya (seeing) and idrak (perception)?
A) Ru'ya is the same as idrak
B) Ru'ya implies idrak
C) Ru'ya does not entail idrak; idrak is additional
D) Idrak is a part of ru'ya

Key Takeaway
Ru'ya (seeing Allah) is possible without full perception (idrak) and without direction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself imagining Allah as having a location in space, even subconsciously? How does this passage challenge that image, and what will you do to train your mind to think of Him without direction?
?
When you think about seeing Allah in the Hereafter, do you secretly feel you would fully comprehend Him? This lesson says you will see but never perceive. Does that humble you? How does it affect your longing for that vision?
?
The sun is seen yet not fully perceived. In what areas of your life do you act as if you have fully grasped Allah—His mercy, His justice, His decree? Where do you need to submit to the fact that He is beyond your full comprehension?

Seeing Allah Without Direction
Author affirms ru'yah by mutawatir hadith and refutes those who claim direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ahl al-Sunnah affirms the vision of Allah in the afterlife without any direction, based on transmitted evidence, and how to correctly interpret the hadith comparing it to seeing the moon.

Core Idea
Seeing Allah is real, but without direction — the moon hadith compares clarity, not location.

This passage confronts a common misunderstanding: some theologians argued that vision requires facing an object and therefore implies direction for Allah. The author firmly corrects this by grounding the belief in the hadith traditions (mutawatir) and clarifying the correct interpretation of the famous moon hadith. Let's examine his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the authority of mutawatir hadith on ru'yah
The author begins by stating that the hadiths on ru'yah are mutawatir in meaning, so they must be accepted based on transmission (naql), not rejected by rational speculation.
This establishes the source of knowledge on this attribute.
2
Identify the mistaken view of the Tahawi commentator
The author criticizes a commentator who argued that vision without facing is rationally impossible, implying direction for Allah.
The commentator's error is conflating vision with direction.
3
Learn the position of Ahl al-Sunnah on ru'yah without direction
The author explicitly states that Allah is not seen in a direction.
This is key to preserving divine transcendence (tanzih).
4
Interpret the moon hadith correctly
The Prophet's comparison is about the clarity and certainty of the vision, not about the seen object being similar to the moon.
This refutes anthropomorphism (tashbih).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the hadiths affirming the vision (al-ru'yah) have been transmitted with overwhelming frequency (tawatur ma'nawi), so it is obligatory to accept them transmittedly, and no attention is paid to what the people of innovation (ahl al-bid'a) imagine rationally. Commentary
This passage addresses the vision of Allah (ru'yah) in the afterlife. The author first asserts that the hadiths proving the vision are mutawatir (massively transmitted) in meaning, so they must be accepted based on transmitted evidence (naql), and one should not heed the rational doubts of the people of innovation. Then he criticizes a commentator of 'Aqidat al-Tahawi who argued that vision without facing is rationally impossible and used it as evidence for Allah's highness above creation. The author implies that this commentator is implying direction (jiha) for Allah, which contradicts the creed of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah that Allah is seen without direction. Finally, the author explains the hadith 'You will see your Lord as you see the moon' by saying it is a comparison of the act of vision to the act of vision (i.e., both are clear and definite), not a comparison of the seen object to the seen object in all aspects, thus refuting anthropomorphism.
