Ruling on Expressing Divine Attributes in Persian
Ruling on translating divine attributes into Persian with exception for 'yad'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the permissibility of translating most divine attributes into Persian, with the specific exception of the attribute 'hand' (yad), and the condition of negating tashbih and kayfiyya when using terms like 'wajh'.

Core Idea
Most divine attributes can be expressed in Persian, except for 'yad' which must remain in Arabic.

This passage from the Manah al-Rawdh al-Azhar addresses a practical question: can divine attributes be translated into Persian? The scholars establish a general ruling with a specific exception, and add a condition for using terms like 'face'. Understanding this nuance helps avoid both linguistic innovation and anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: a general statement, an exception, and a special case with conditions.
2
Identify the general ruling and the exception
The passage states that all attributes mentioned by scholars in Persian are permissible except for 'yad' (hand). Find the phrase that expresses this exception.
The exception is 'sawa al-yad bi al-farisiyya'.
3
Understand the condition for using 'wajh'
The passage also permits saying 'بروى خدا' (face of God) but only when free from tashbih (likening) and kayfiyya (modality). Reflect on why this condition is necessary.
This prevents anthropomorphism while allowing translation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكل ما ذكره العلماء بالفارسية من صفات الله تعالى عزت أسماؤه وتعالت صفاته فجائز القول به سوى اليد بالفارسية، ويجوز أن يقال: «بروى خدا»، بلا تشبيه ولا كيفية. وكل ما وفي نسخة: 'وكل شيء' ذكره العلماء (بالفارسية) أي بغير العبارة العربية من صفات الله (تعالى أي المتشابهة كالوجه والقدم والعين. وفي نسخة: 'من صفات الباري' عزت أسماؤه أي غلبت على الأفهام وتعالت صفاته أي ارتفعت عن الأوهام فجائز القول به أي بأن نتبعهم في التعبير عن أسمائه وصفاته حسب وصفاته حسب ما ذكره العلماء باختلاف لغاته سوى اليد (بالفارسية أي فإنه لا يجوز تعبيرها بالفارسية كما في نسخة: أي بغير عبارة وردت في الكتاب والسنة، ومفهومه أنه يجوز للعلماء وغيرهم أن يعبروا في صفته ونعته بذكر اليد ونحوها على وفق ما ورد بها كما يقال بيده أزمة التحقيق، والله ولي التوفيق. ويتفرع على الحصر المذكور بالوجه المسطور قوله : ويجوز أن يقال: «بروى خدا» بضم الراء وسكون الواو أي وجه الله بلا تشبيه ولا كيفية أي مقرونا بنفي التشبيه والكيفية.
And everything that the scholars have mentioned in Persian from the attributes of Allah Most High—may His names be exalted and His attributes be transcendent—it is permissible to say it, except for 'hand' (al-yad) in Persian. And it is permissible to say: 'بروى خدا' (face of God) without likening (tashbih) or modality (kayfiyya). And everything that, and in a copy: 'everything' that the scholars mentioned in Persian, i.e., in other than Arabic expression, from the attributes of Allah Most High, i.e., the ambiguous ones like the face, the foot, and the eye. And in a copy: 'from the attributes of the Creator'—may His names be exalted, i.e., they dominate over understandings, and His attributes be transcendent, i.e., they are elevated above imaginations—so it is permissible to say it, i.e., that we follow them in expressing His names and attributes according to what the scholars mentioned with different languages, except for 'hand' in Persian, i.e., it is not permissible to express it in Persian, as in a copy: i.e., other than an expression that came in the Book and the Sunnah. And its meaning is that it is permissible for scholars and others to express in His attribute and description by mentioning 'hand' and the like in accordance with what has been reported, as it is said 'in His hand are the reins of verification'—and Allah is the grantor of success. And it branches from the aforementioned limitation by the recorded statement: 'and it is permissible to say: 'بروى خدا' with a damm on the ra and sukun on the waw, i.e., face of Allah, without tashbih or kayfiyya,' i.e., accompanied by negation of tashbih and kayfiyya.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on expressing divine attributes in Persian. It begins by stating a general principle: whatever attributes scholars have expressed in Persian, it is permissible to say, except for the attribute 'yad' (hand), which may not be rendered in Persian. It then adds that saying 'بروى خدا' (face of God) is permissible, but only when it is free from tashbih (likening to creation) and takyif (asking 'how'). The commentary then elaborates: the attributes in question are the ambiguous (mutashabihat) ones like face, foot, and eye. The phrase 'عزت أسماؤه' is explained as their dominance over minds, and 'تعالت صفاته' as their being above imaginations. The permissibility means we follow the scholars in using different languages for these expressions, but once again the exception of 'yad' is stressed—it cannot be translated into Persian, because it must be expressed only in the Arabic terms from the Qur'an and Sunnah. However, the commentary clarifies that it is permissible for scholars and others to use the word 'yad' in Arabic phrases like 'biyadihi azimat al-tahqiq' (in His hand are the reins of verification) as long as it follows what has been reported. Then it returns to the clause about 'wajh' (face): 'بروى خدا' is permissible only with the condition of denying tashbih and kayfiyya.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all divine attributes can be freely translated into any language without restriction.
Correction: The passage shows that 'yad' (hand) is an exception and cannot be expressed in Persian; it must remain in Arabic.

Checkpoint
What is the exception regarding Persian translations of Allah's attributes?
A) All attributes can be translated.
B) Only 'yad' (hand) cannot be translated.
C) Only 'wajh' (face) cannot be translated.
D) None can be translated.

Key Takeaway
Divine attributes may be expressed in Persian except for 'yad', and terms like 'wajh' are permitted only when stripped of anthropomorphic implications.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the phrase 'yad Allah' (hand of Allah) in the Qur'an, does your heart remain still without trying to picture anything? This lesson commands you to say it only as reported—no translation, no imagination. Have you ever felt a subtle desire to explain 'how' it is? If so, are you willing to discipline that desire and simply submit to 'billā kayf' (without how)?
?

Is your speech about Allah's attributes always paired with this negation? Or do you sometimes speak of His face, hand, or other attributes while forgetting to guard your heart from subtle anthropomorphism? Repent from every careless utterance and commit to using only the revealed expressions with the protective clauses.
?

Or do you recognize that some words—like 'yad'—are sacred containers of meaning that cannot be replaced without losing their exact revealed sense? What change will you make today in how you speak about Allah to ensure you honor His names and attributes?

Allah's Nearness and Distance: Beyond Space and Status
Clarification that divine nearness/distance are neither spatial nor honorific, but affirmed without how.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah's nearness and distance are real but not spatial or metaphorical; they must be affirmed without 'how' (bila kayf) to avoid anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
Allah's nearness is without how; neither space nor honor.

A common confusion when reading verses like 'My mercy is near' is to imagine physical nearness. Imam Abu Hanifa clarifies that both nearness and distance of Allah are not about spatial length or worldly honor. Instead, the obedient is near without how, and the disobedient is far without how. This lesson establishes the correct understanding of these attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Let the author's own words establish the framework before analyzing.
Notice the repetition of 'bila kayf' – this is the key term.
2
Identify what nearness and distance are NOT
The passage explicitly negates two things: spatial distance (length) and worldly honor/humiliation. Make a mental note of these negations.
This guards against both anthropomorphism and metaphorical over-interpretation.
3
Understand the positive meaning: affirm without how
Having negated false meanings, the passage affirms that the obedient are near to Allah 'without how', and the disobedient are far 'without how'. Reflect on the significance of 'bila kayf' in preserving divine transcendence.
This is the core of the lesson: real but unknowable modality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس قرب الله تعالى ولا بعده من طريق طول المسافة وقصرها ولا على معنى الكرامة والهوان، ولكن المطيع قريب منه بلا كيف، والعاصي بعيد عنه بلا كيف، والقرب والبعد والإقبال يقع على المناجي، وليس قرب الله تعالى أي من أرباب الطاعة ولا بعده أي من أصحاب المعصية كما في الحديث: ((إن السخي قريب من الله والبخيل بعيد من الله)). من طريق طول المسافة أي الحسية المعبر عنها بالمساحة وقصرها بل المراد بهما القرب والبعد المعنوي كما يستفاد من منطوق قوله سبحانه: ﴿إِنَّ رَحْمَتَ اللهِ قَرِيبٌ مِنَ الْمُحْسِنِينَ ﴾ [الأعراف:٥٦] المفهوم منه اللهِ أنه بعيد من المسيئين ولا على معنى الكرامة والهوان أي وليسا محمولين على معنى الكرامة والإحسان والذلة والهوان فإن هذا تأويل في مقام أهل العرفان والإمام الأعظم رحمه الله تعالى جعلهما من باب المتشابه في مقام الإيقان، ولذا قال : (ولكن المطيع قريب منه بلا كيف أي من غير التشبيه والعاصي بعيد عنه بلا كيف أي بوصف التنزيه والقرب والبعد والإقبال أي وضده وهو الإعراض يقع على المناجي أي يطلق أيضاً على العبد المتضرع إلى الله، المتذلل لديه طالبا لرضاه كما في قوله تعالى: وَاسْجُدْ وَاقْتَرِبْ [العلق : ١٩]، أي اسجد لله وتقرب إلى رضاه . وقيل : دُم على السجود والتقرب إلى الله ثم رأيت السلف أجمعوا على عدم تأويل اليد وتبعهم الأشعري في ذلك، بخلاف سائر الصفات، فإن فيها خلافاً عنهم بالتأويل والتفويض.
And the nearness of Allah Most High and His distance are not by way of length of distance and shortness, nor on the meaning of honor and humiliation; rather, the obedient one is near to Him without how (bila kayf), and the disobedient one is far from Him without how. And nearness, distance, and facing are spoken of in regard to the one who supplicates. 'And the nearness of Allah Most High' means from the people of obedience, and 'His distance' means from the people of disobedience, as in the hadith: 'Indeed, the generous is near to Allah and the miser is far from Allah.' 'By way of length of distance' means the sensory one expressed by measurement and its shortness, but what is meant by them is the spiritual nearness and distance, as understood from the explicit wording of His statement: 'Indeed, the mercy of Allah is near to the doers of good' [al-A'raf:56], from which it is understood that He is far from the evildoers. 'Nor on the meaning of honor and humiliation' means they are not carried on the meaning of honor and beneficence and abasement and humiliation, for that is interpretation (ta'wil) in the station of the people of gnosis; and the Great Imam (Abu Hanifa), may Allah have mercy on him, made them part of the ambiguous (mutashabih) in the station of certainty. Hence he said: 'But the obedient one is near to Him without how,' i.e., without tashbih, 'and the disobedient is far from Him without how,' i.e., with the description of transcendence. And 'nearness, distance, and facing'—i.e., and its opposite, which is turning away—'are spoken of in regard to the one who supplicates,' i.e., they are also applied to the servant who prays humbly to Allah, abasing himself before Him, seeking His pleasure, as in His statement: 'Prostrate and draw near' [al-'Alaq:19], i.e., prostrate to Allah and draw near to His pleasure. And it is said: persist in prostration and drawing near to Allah. Then I saw that the Salaf agreed on not interpreting 'yad' (the hand) and al-Ash'ari followed them in that, contrary to other attributes, concerning which there is a difference among them between ta'wil and tafwid.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the terms 'qurb' (nearness) and 'bu'd' (distance) as applied to Allah. It is a doctrinal clarification. First, it negates that nearness or distance is physical or spatial (length of distance), nor is it about worldly honor or humiliation. Rather, the obedient believer is 'near' to Allah without a 'how' (bila kayf), and the disobedient is 'far' without a 'how'. The commentary expands: the nearness is spiritual, not spatial, citing a hadith about the generous and miser, and the verse 'My mercy is near to the doers of good'. It clarifies that these terms are not to be interpreted as honor or humiliation in the human sense because that would be tahrif or ta'wil inappropriate for ordinary believers; Imam Abu Hanifa classified them as mutashabihat (ambiguous). Then the commentary returns to the original statement: 'the obedient is near without how' means without tashbih, and the disobedient is far with the description of tanzih (transcendence). It also says that nearness, distance, and 'iqbal' (facing) are used for the one who supplicates (munaji), i.e., the servant humbling himself before Allah, as in the verse 'Prostrate and draw near'. Finally, the passage adds a historical note: the Salaf (early generations) unanimously refrained from ta'wil (interpretation) of the attribute 'yad' (hand), and al-Ash'ari followed them, but for other attributes there was difference between ta'wil and tafwid (consigning the meaning to Allah).

Key Terms
القرب
البعد
بلا كيف
التشبيه
المطيع
العاصي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that Allah's nearness is physical closeness or that it means worldly honor.
Correction: The passage clarifies that nearness is not spatial length nor worldly honor, but a spiritual reality without 'how'.

Checkpoint
How should the nearness of Allah to the obedient be understood according to the passage?
A) As spatial nearness.
B) As a nearness without a 'how' (bila kayf) and without tashbih.
C) As an increase in honor and status like worldly honor.
D) As both spatial and metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
The obedient are near to Allah without how, and the disobedient are far from Him without how; this protects tawhid from tashbih and ta'til.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel distant from Allah because of your sins, do you imagine that distance as a kind of spiritual punishment or as Allah's displeasure? How does this lesson's teaching that distance is 'without how' challenge you to rethink your feelings of alienation and instead turn to repentance without despair?
?
You may have sought Allah's nearness through acts of worship, but have you ever evaluated your own obedience based on emotional or sensory experiences of closeness? According to this lesson, nearness is true and certain even without feeling it. Will you commit to trusting the reality of His nearness even when your heart feels cold?
?
Consider a time you felt honored or humiliated by worldly circumstances. The lesson says divine nearness is not honor and distance is not humiliation. Are you willing to detach your sense of worth before Allah from your worldly status, and instead measure your standing solely by obedience and disobedience, without how?

Nearness for the Supplicant and the Salaf on the Hand of Allah
Explanation that nearness/distance/facing apply to the supplicant, and Salaf's practice on tafwid of yad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that nearness, distance, and facing are also used for the supplicant, and that the Salaf refrained from interpreting the attribute 'yad' (hand), leaving its meaning to Allah.

Core Idea
The supplicant is near in supplication; the hand of Allah is not interpreted, but consigned.

The previous lesson established that Allah's nearness and distance are without how. Now the passage explains that these terms are also applied to the servant who prays humbly, as in the command 'Prostrate and draw near.' It then discusses the methodology of the Salaf regarding the attribute 'yad' (hand), showing that they did not interpret it, unlike some other attributes. This lesson connects the concept of divine attributes to the practice of the early Muslims.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on nearness for the supplicant
Focus on the part where 'nearness, distance, and facing' are said to apply to the one who supplicates (al-munaji). Note the verse cited: 'Prostrate and draw near.'
Drawing near here means drawing near to Allah's pleasure, not spatial proximity.
2
Study the Salaf's position on 'yad'
The passage states that the Salaf unanimously refrained from interpreting 'yad' (hand), and al-Ash'ari followed them. Understand the difference between ta'wil (interpretation) and tafwid (consigning the meaning) for other attributes.
This shows a methodological distinction: some attributes are left uninterpreted, others may have interpretation.
3
Reflect on the practical lesson
Consider how this passage guides the believer: when encountering attributes like 'yad', we should follow the Salaf in tafwid, avoiding both tashbih and excessive ta'wil.
This is a key principle in Sunni theology.

Salaf's Consensus on the Hand Attribute
Author states Salaf's consensus on not interpreting hand and Ash'ari's following.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Salaf's nuanced approach to ambiguous divine attributes, specifically their consensus on not interpreting the hand, and the difference of opinion on other attributes.

Core Idea
The Salaf agreed not to interpret the Hand but differed on other attributes.

This passage from a classical commentary clarifies a subtle methodological point: the Salaf did not treat all ambiguous attributes identically. While they unanimously avoided interpreting the attribute of 'hand', they allowed interpretation or delegation for other attributes. Let us see how the author presents this consensus and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then the translation. Notice the contrast between the hand and other attributes.
2
Identify the consensus
The passage states that the Salaf unanimously agreed not to interpret the hand. Also note that al-Ash'ari followed them.
Consensus (ijma) is a key source of authority in Sunni theology.
3
Understand the distinction
Contrast this with other attributes, where the Salaf differed between interpretation (ta'wil) and delegation (tafwid).
This shows a nuanced methodology, not a blanket rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كان القول مقرونا بالتنزيه ونفي التشبيه فالفرق بين اليد والوجه تدقيق يحتاج إلى تحقيق، ثم رأيت السلف أجمعوا على عدم تأويل اليد وتبعهم الأشعري في ذلك، بخلاف سائر الصفات، فإن فيها خلافاً عنهم بالتأويل والتفويض.
And when speech is accompanied by transcendence and negation of anthropomorphism, then the distinction between the hand and the face is a subtlety that needs investigation. Then I saw that the Salaf have unanimously agreed on not interpreting the hand, and al-Ash'ari followed them in that, contrary to the rest of the attributes, for there is a difference of opinion from them regarding interpretation and delegation.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is a methodological statement about handling the ambiguous attributes of Allah (mutashabihat). The author notes that when the discourse is already grounded in tanzih (transcendence) and tashbih (anthropomorphism) is negated, then making a distinction between the attribute of 'hand' (yad) and 'face' (wajh) is a subtle point that requires careful investigation. He then observes that the Salaf (early righteous predecessors) were unanimous in not interpreting (ta'til) the attribute of hand, meaning they accepted it without determining its modality. Imam al-Ash'ari followed them in this stance. However, for the other attributes (sifat), there is disagreement among the Salaf: some practiced ta'wil (figurative interpretation) while others practiced tafwid (consigning the meaning to Allah). This shows a nuanced methodology where not all attributes are treated identically.

Key Terms
يد
وجه
تأويل
تفويض
سلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Salaf interpreted all ambiguous attributes the same way.
Correction: This passage shows they distinguished between the hand/face and other attributes, with a consensus on the former and difference on the latter.

Checkpoint
What is the Salaf's consensus regarding the attribute of 'hand' (yad)?
A) They unanimously interpreted it figuratively.
B) They unanimously agreed not to interpret it.
C) They unanimously delegated its meaning to Allah.
D) They disagreed on it.

Key Takeaway
The Salaf unanimously refrained from interpreting the attribute of 'hand', while they differed on other attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you come across an attribute of Allah that your mind cannot fully grasp, do you feel compelled to reinterpret it until it 'makes sense', or can you humbly accept it as it is, trusting in His transcendence? What does your response reveal about your heart's submission to divine revelation?
?
The Salaf unanimously left the hand attribute uninterpreted, yet we find people today insisting on explanations for similar attributes. Is there an aspect of your faith where you demand intellectual closure instead of surrendering to Allah's words? How can you cultivate the same trust as the Salaf?
?
This lesson shows that not all ambiguous attributes are treated the same: the hand has consensus, other attributes have differences. Does this nuance challenge your assumption that one approach fits all? How will you respond when you encounter scholarly disagreement on divine attributes—will you seek to understand the evidence or take a rigid stance?

The Equality and Double Virtue of Quranic Verses
Classification of Quranic verses by virtue of mention and mentioned

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that all Quranic verses are equal in virtue as divine speech, but some have an additional virtue due to the excellence of what they mention, such as Ayat al-Kursi.

Core Idea
All Quranic verses are equal in speech; some are doubly blessed by their subject matter.

This passage from a classical creedal work clarifies a subtle point about the Quran's verses. While all verses are equally the speech of Allah and share the same fundamental virtue, some verses carry an extra dimension of excellence because of the content they describe. The author uses Ayat al-Kursi as the prime example, showing how it combines the virtue of mentioning Allah with the virtue of the glorious attributes it contains.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the key terms: فضيلة الذكر (virtue of the mention) and فضيلة المذكور (virtue of the mentioned).
The passage is concise; each sentence carries doctrinal weight.
2
Identify the two categories of verses
The passage divides verses into two groups: those with only the virtue of the mention (like verses describing disbelievers), and those with both virtues (like Ayat al-Kursi). Find the criteria for this division.
The division is based on the content – what is being mentioned.
3
Understand the double virtue of Ayat al-Kursi
Ayat al-Kursi mentions the majesty, greatness, and attributes of Allah. Thus it has both the virtue of the act of mentioning (which all verses share) and the virtue of the subject mentioned (Allah's glory). Trace how the text explains this.
This is the key insight: the subject of the verse can add a layer of excellence.
4
Contrast with verses about disbelievers
Verses that describe disbelievers only have the virtue of the mention, because the subject (the disbelievers) has no virtue. This contrast clarifies the principle.
The virtue of the mention is always present; the extra virtue depends on the subject.
5
Reflect on the overall lesson
Consider why this distinction matters. It affirms the Quran's unity as divine speech while acknowledging a secondary hierarchy based on content, without contradicting the Quran's perfection.
This balances reverence for the entire Quran with the special status of certain verses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقرآن منزل على رسول الله، وهو في المصحف مكتوب، وآيات القرآن كلها في معنى الكلام مستوية في الفضيلة والعظمة إلا أن لبعضها فضيلة الذكر وفضيلة المذكور، مثل آية الكرسي؛ لأن المذكور فيها جلال الله تعالى وعظمته وصفته، فاجتمعت فيها فضيلتان فضيلة الذكر وفضيلة المذكور، وفي صفة الكفار فضيلة الذكر فحسب، وليس في المذكور وهم الكفار فضيلة.
And the Quran is sent down upon the Messenger of Allah, and it is written in the mus'haf, and all the verses of the Quran are equal in virtue and greatness in the meaning of speech, except that some of them have the virtue of the mention and the virtue of the mentioned, such as the Verse of the Throne; because what is mentioned in it is the majesty of Allah Almighty, His greatness and His attribute, so two virtues are combined in it: the virtue of the mention and the virtue of the mentioned. And in the description of the disbelievers, there is only the virtue of the mention, and there is no virtue in the mentioned, namely the disbelievers.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal statement on the status of Quranic verses. It affirms that all verses are equal in virtue and greatness insofar as they are divine speech, but then introduces an exception: some verses have an additional virtue due to the content they mention. The example given is Ayat al-Kursi, which mentions the majesty, greatness, and attributes of Allah, thus combining the virtue of the act of mentioning (the words) and the virtue of the subject mentioned (Allah's glory). In contrast, verses that describe the disbelievers have only the virtue of the mention itself, because the subject mentioned (the disbelievers) has no inherent virtue. This distinction preserves the fundamental equality of the Quran as speech while acknowledging a secondary excellence based on content.

Key Terms
آيات القرآن
فضيلة الذكر
فضيلة المذكور
آية الكرسي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some verses are simply more virtuous than others in all respects, implying a lack of perfection in some Quranic speech.
Correction: The equality is in the meaning of speech (في معنى الكلام); all verses are equally divine speech. The extra virtue is not a deficiency in others but an additional merit due to the subject mentioned.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does Ayat al-Kursi have double virtue?
A) Because it is the longest verse in the Quran.
B) Because it mentions the majesty, greatness and attributes of Allah.
C) Because it was revealed directly without an angel.
D) Because it contains the name of Allah.

Key Takeaway
All Quranic verses are equal as divine speech, but some gain an additional virtue from the excellence of their subject matter, like Ayat al-Kursi.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite Ayat al-Kursi, do you ever pause to reflect on the majesty of the One you are describing? Or do you rush through it, missing the double blessing? What would change if you recited it with the awareness that you are both speaking divine words and contemplating the Divine Himself?
?
Consider a verse in the Quran that describes the disbelievers. It still has the virtue of mention. Do you show the same reverence when reciting such verses as you do with verses about Allah's attributes? Why or why not? What does your attitude reveal about your understanding of the Quran's unity?
?
The author says all verses are equal in speech. Yet we often rank verses by our preferences. How can you cultivate equal reverence for every word of the Quran, knowing that each is from Allah, while still appreciating the extra virtue of some? What practical step can you take today to honor a verse you usually overlook?

The Equality of Allah's Names and Attributes in Virtue
Statement that Allah's names and attributes are equal in virtue and greatness with a clarification about the Greatest Name.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that all of Allah's names and attributes are equal in their essential application to His essence, yet some may be greater in virtue as indicated by revealed texts.

Core Idea
Allah's names and attributes are equal in essence but not in revealed merit.

This passage extends the previous discussion on Quranic verses to the divine names and attributes. It establishes that in their fundamental attribution to Allah, all names and attributes are alike in virtue and greatness. However, the author immediately notes that this does not conflict with the hadith teaching that some names are greater than others, such as the Greatest Name.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses parenthetical explanations within the text.
2
Identify the two main claims
The passage makes a statement of equality and then a clarification about superiority. Find both and note how the author connects them.
The key phrase is 'وهو لا ينافي' which signals the clarification.
3
Understand the distinction
Reflect on what 'equality in structure and meaning' means. The author says equality applies to both the form (mabnā) and the meaning (maʿnā) when applying names and attributes to Allah.
This is the core of the argument.
4
Connect to hadith on the Greatest Name
Think about why the author brings up the Greatest Name. How does the equality principle relate to the hadiths that praise some names more than others?
The clarification resolves a potential contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك الأسماء والصفات كلها مستوية في الفضيلة والعظمة لا تفاوت بينهما وكذلك الأسماء) أي نحو الله الأحد الصمد الملك الواحد الفرد (والصفات) أي نحو: له الملك وله الحمد وله الكبرياء والمجد كلها مستوية في الفضيلة) أي بحسب المبنى (والعظمة) أي باعتبار المعنى لا) تفاوت بينهما) أي من حيث إطلاقهما على ذاته وصفاته كليهما، وهو لا ينافي أن يكون بعض الأسماء وبعض الصفات أعظم من بعضها على ما ثبت في الأحاديث الواردة في فضل الاسم الأعظم، والله تعالى أعلم.
And likewise, the names and attributes are all equal in virtue and greatness; there is no difference between them. So the names (such as Allah, al-Ahad, al-Samad, al-Malik, al-Wahid, al-Fard) and the attributes (such as: to Him belongs the dominion, to Him belongs praise, to Him belongs pride and glory) are all equal in virtue (i.e., with respect to structure) and greatness (i.e., with respect to meaning); there is no difference between them, meaning in terms of their application to His essence and His attributes, both. And this does not contradict that some names and some attributes are greater than others, as established in the hadiths about the virtue of the Greatest Name. And Allah Almighty knows best.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage extends the previous discussion on Quranic verses to Allah's names and attributes. It asserts that all names and attributes of Allah are equal in virtue and greatness in their intrinsic quality as referring to His essence. The author distinguishes between the structural (mabnā) and meaningful (ma'nā) aspects, stating equality in both. However, he immediately qualifies this by noting that it is not contradictory to hold that some names or attributes are greater than others in certain respects, as indicated by hadith on the Greatest Name (al-ism al-aʿẓam). Thus, the equality is in the essential application to Allah, while relative excellence based on revealed merits is also affirmed.

Key Terms

الصفات
الاسم الأعظم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If all names are equal, then no name can be greater than another.
Correction: The passage clarifies that equality is in essential application to Allah, not in all aspects; revealed hadith indicate some names are greater in merit, such as the Greatest Name.

Checkpoint
What does the passage say about the relationship between the equality of names/attributes and the hadith about the Greatest Name?
A) The hadith contradicts the equality, so it must be rejected.
B) The equality is in essence, but some names may be greater in other respects as per hadith.
C) The Greatest Name is not among the names of Allah.
D) The equality means all names are exactly the same in every way.

Key Takeaway
Allah's names and attributes are all equal in their essential reference to Him, yet some are distinguished in virtue by divine revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah by His names, do you tend to repeat only a few favorites? How would your dua change if you truly believed that every name is equal in its essential reference to Him?
?
Have you ever felt that one attribute of Allah (like His Mercy) is more important to you than another (like His Justice)? How does this lesson challenge you to honor all of His attributes equally in your heart?
?
If you learned that the Greatest Name is hidden, does that make you anxious or trust that Allah knows what is best? What would it mean for you to approach all His names with the same reverence as the Greatest Name?

4 min
Equality of Divine Names and Attributes
Commentary explaining equality of names/attributes and the exception of the Greatest Name.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the divine names and attributes are equal in virtue and greatness, and that this equality does not preclude the existence of a 'Greatest Name' as affirmed by hadith.

Core Idea
All divine names and attributes are equal in greatness, yet some are greater.

The commentator builds on the matn's statement to explain what it means for names and attributes to be equal in virtue and greatness. He clarifies that this equality applies to both the form and meaning when applied to Allah, and then addresses a potential question: does this contradict the hadith about the Greatest Name? This passage resolves that by showing that equality does not negate gradation in rank.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the commentator's flow come through.
Notice the structure: a main claim followed by an exception.
2
Identify the main claim about equality
The commentator affirms that all names and attributes are equal in virtue and greatness. Notice the two aspects: form (al-mabna) and meaning (al-ma‘na).
3
Notice the clarification about the Greatest Name
The commentator adds that this equality does not contradict the hadiths about some names being greater, especially the Greatest Name.
This is the key point: equality and gradation can coexist.
4
Reflect on the balance
Ask yourself how the commentator maintains both equality and superiority. The answer lies in the distinction between essence (no disparity) and rank (some are greater).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك الأسماء والصفات كلها مستوية في الفضيلة والعظمة لا تفاوت بينهما وكذلك الأسماء) أي نحو الله الأحد الصمد الملك الواحد الفرد (والصفات) أي نحو: له الملك وله الحمد وله الكبرياء والمجد كلها مستوية في الفضيلة) أي بحسب المبنى (والعظمة) أي باعتبار المعنى لا) تفاوت بينهما) أي من حيث إطلاقهما على ذاته وصفاته كليهما، وهو لا ينافي أن يكون بعض الأسماء وبعض الصفات أعظم من بعضها على ما ثبت في الأحاديث الواردة في فضل الاسم الأعظم، والله تعالى أعلم.
And likewise the names and attributes, all of them, are equal in virtue (al-fadila) and greatness (al-‘azama), no disparity between them. (And likewise the names) i.e., like Allah, al-Ahad, al-Samad, al-Malik, al-Wahid, al-Fard, (and the attributes) i.e., like: to Him belongs the kingdom, to Him belongs praise, to Him belongs pride and glory – all of them are equal in virtue (al-fadila) i.e., with respect to form (al-mabna), and in greatness (al-‘azama) i.e., with respect to meaning (al-ma‘na). No disparity between them, i.e., with respect to their application to His essence and His attributes, both of them. And this does not contradict that some names and some attributes are greater than others, as established in the hadiths reported concerning the virtue of the Greatest Name. And Allah Most High knows best.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a previous matn, explaining the statement that the divine names and attributes are all equal in virtue and greatness. The commentator clarifies that the equality is in terms of both form (al-mabna) and meaning (al-ma‘na), and that there is no disparity when they are applied to God's essence and attributes. However, he adds that this does not contradict the fact that some names and attributes are greater than others, as evidenced by the hadiths about the Greatest Name (ism Allah al-a‘zam). Thus, the passage balances a general equality of all names and attributes with the possibility of some being superior in rank.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That equality of names and attributes means no name can be greater than another.
Correction: The passage clarifies that equality in virtue and greatness does not negate the existence of a 'Greatest Name' as per hadith.

Checkpoint

B) That names and attributes are different in essence
C) That there is no disparity at all
D) That attributes are greater than names

Key Takeaway
All divine names and attributes are equal in greatness, yet some are greater, as taught by the Greatest Name tradition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah by His names, do you ever reflect that every name is equally worthy of His majesty, or do you treat some as more powerful only when you need something? How does this lesson correct your attitude?
?

?
The passage separates equality in form and meaning. In your own worship, do you pay attention to both the form (the words you say) and the meaning (the reality you intend), or do you neglect one? Which one are you more prone to neglect, and what will you change today?

The Obligation of the Impossible: Arguments and Critique
Hujjat al-Islam argues for permissibility, Ibn al-Humam critiques.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key arguments for and against the permissibility of God obligating what is beyond a person's capacity, as debated by Hujjat al-Islam and Ibn al-Humam.

Core Idea
God's ability to obligate the impossible is defended by Quranic and logical arguments, but faces nuanced critique.

This lesson delves into one of the more intricate topics in kalam: can God command a person to do something they are completely unable to do? Hujjat al-Islam (al-Ghazali) presents two proofs for permissibility, one from a Quranic prayer and another from a logical paradox involving Abu Jahl. Ibn al-Humam then offers a sharp critique of the first proof, distinguishing between different types of impositions. By examining these arguments, we gain insight into the positions of the Ash'aris, Mu'tazila, and Hanafis on divine justice and human capacity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text slowly, then read the English translation. Notice how Hujjat al-Islam frames his argument and how Ibn al-Humam responds.
The passage is dense with theological reasoning.
2
Identify Hujjat al-Islam's two proofs
Find the first proof based on the Quranic verse (2:286) and the second proof based on the case of Abu Jahl (or Abu Lahab). Understand how each is meant to show that obligating the impossible is possible.
The second proof involves a paradox: God informs that someone will not believe, yet commands that person to believe all statements, including the statement about his disbelief.
3
Understand Ibn al-Humam's critique
Ibn al-Humam argues that the first proof does not address the real issue. He explains what the Mu'tazila, Hanafis, and Ash'aris actually hold regarding impossible burdens. Pay attention to his distinctions.
He differentiates between causing death by imposing a mountain (which is permissible) and obligating with punishment for non-compliance (the real point of dispute).
4
Reflect on the sectarian positions
Note how the Mu'tazila, Hanafis, and Ash'aris each deal with this issue. The passage shows that the dispute is not about God's power but about the logical coherence of punishment for an impossible act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من فروع هذا الأصل ما ذكره حجة الإسلام حيث قال: يجوز الله أن يكلف عباده ما لا يطيقونه خلافا للمعتزلة؛ إذ لو لم يجز لاستحال سؤال دفعه وقد سألوا ذلك فقالوا: رَبَّنَا وَلَا تُحَمِّلْنَا مَا لَا طَاقَةَ لَنَا بِهِ ﴾ [البقرة : ٢٨٦] ؛ ولأنه سبحانه أخبر أن أبا جهل لا يصدقه عليه الصلاة والسلام ثم أمره أن يصدق بجميع أقواله عليه الصلاة والسلام ومن جملتها أنه لا يصدقه عليه الصلاة والسلام فكيف يصدقه عليه الصلاة والسلام في أنه لا يصدقه؟ هذا محال . انتهى . وذكره غيره أنه قال أبو لهب بدل أبي جهل وهو أنسب. قال ابن الهمام ولا يخفى أن الدليل الأول ليس في محل النزاع، وهو التكليف، إذ عند القائلين بامتناعه يجوز أي يحمله جبلا فيموت، وأما عند المعتزلة فبناء على جواز أنواع الإيلام بقصد العوض وجوبا، وأما عند الحنفية المانعين منه أيضاً فتفضلا بحكم وعده على المصائب، ولا يجوز أن يكلفه أن يحمل جبلا بحيث إذا لم يفعل يعاقب، أي وجوزه الأشاعرة كما قال الله تعالى: لا يُكَلِّفُ اللهُ نَفْسًا إِلَّا وُسْعَهَا) [البقرة: ٢٨٦] ، وعن هذا النص ذهب المحققون ممن.
Then from the branches of this principle is what Hujjat al-Islam mentioned, where he said: God may obligate His servants with what they cannot bear, contrary to the Mu'tazila. For if it were not permissible, then asking to ward it off would be impossible, yet they asked that, saying: 'Our Lord, and do not burden us with what we cannot bear' [Quran 2:286]. And because He, exalted, informed that Abu Jahl would not believe him, peace and blessings be upon him, then He commanded him to believe in all his statements, peace be upon him, and among them is that he would not believe him. So how can he believe him regarding the fact that he does not believe him? This is impossible. End. And someone else mentioned that he said Abu Lahab instead of Abu Jahl, and that is more appropriate. Ibn al-Humam said: It is not hidden that the first proof is not about the subject of dispute, which is taklif (obligation). For according to those who deem it impossible, it is permissible that He cause him to carry a mountain so that he dies. As for the Mu'tazila, it is based on the permissibility of types of infliction of pain with the intention of obligatory compensation. As for the Hanafis who prohibit it as well, it is as a favor by virtue of His promise regarding calamities. And He may not obligate him to carry a mountain such that if he does not do it he is punished. That is, the Ash'aris allowed it, as God said: 'God does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' [Quran 2:286]. And from this text, the verifiers among those who...
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage discusses a detailed theological dispute: whether God can obligate a person to do something impossible (taklif ma la yutaq). Hujjat al-Islam (al-Ghazali) argues that it is permissible, contrary to the Mu'tazila. He gives two proofs: 1) The Quranic verse where believers ask God not to burden them beyond their capacity (2:286) implies that such a burden is possible in principle, otherwise the request would be meaningless. 2) God informed Abu Jahl (or Abu Lahab) that he would not believe the Prophet, yet commanded him to believe all the Prophet's statements, which includes the statement that he would not believe. This creates an impossible command—to believe a statement that says he does not believe. Since God commanded it, it proves the permissibility of obligating the impossible. Ibn al-Humam then critiques the first proof, arguing that it does not address the real point of dispute. For those who deny the possibility, the 'burden' could be interpreted as causing him to die by carrying a mountain (not an impossible obligation). The Mu'tazila allow infliction of pain for compensation. The Hanafis who also forbid it allow it as a favor based on God's promise regarding calamities. The real issue is obligating something with punishment for non-compliance, which the Ash'aris allowed. He cites the verse 'God does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' as the basis for the verifiers' position.

Key Terms
تكليف ما لا يطاق
الجواز
الاستحالة
أبو جهل
أبو لهب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because God is omnipotent, He can obligate a person to do impossible things without any constraints.
Correction: The debate centers on the logical coherence of punishment for non-compliance when the act is impossible. The passage shows that even opponents allow some forms of impossible burdens (like causing death), but the real dispute is about the justice of punishment for an act beyond one's ability.

Checkpoint
What is Hujjat al-Islam's second proof for the permissibility of obligating the impossible?
A) The verse about God not burdening beyond capacity.
B) The case of Abu Jahl being commanded to believe while God informed he would not.
C) The consensus of scholars.
D) The request of the believers in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of obligating the impossible is defended by Hujjat al-Islam using Quranic and logical proofs, but Ibn al-Humam's critique shows the debate hinges on the nature of the burden and the justice of punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Our Lord, do not burden us with what we cannot bear' in your prayers, do you truly feel your dependence on His mercy? Or has it become a mere routine? This lesson shows that the very prayer implies the possibility of such a burden. Are you living with the awareness that God could test you beyond your limits, and are you preparing for that? Let this dua awaken genuine humility in your heart today.
?
The paradox of Abu Jahl: he was commanded to believe something that contradicted his own reality. Have you ever faced a command from Allah that seemed to conflict with your logic or desires? This lesson challenges you to consider: when your mind rebels against a divine command, will you still submit? Reflect on a specific area of your life where you resist full submission. What would it cost you to obey completely?
?
Ibn al-Humam's critique distinguishes between different types of burdens: some lead to reward, others to punishment. Think about the hardships you are currently facing. Are you reacting as if God is unjustly burdening you, or are you recognizing that He may be testing you for a greater good? And if you are failing to obey due to perceived difficulty, are you using that as an excuse? This lesson demands that you examine the intentions behind your complaints and your actions.

God's Freedom in Inflicting Pain
Refutes Mu'tazila view on divine infliction of pain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ash'aris affirm that God can inflict pain without prior sin or compensation, contra the Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
God's actions are never unjust; nothing is obligatory upon Him.

This lesson tackles a subsidiary issue where Ash'aris part ways with the Mu'tazila: can God inflict pain on His creation without any preceding sin or later reward? The Mu'tazila argue this would be unjust, but the Ash'ari response rests on the foundational principle that nothing is obligatory on God. Let's read the passage and unpack the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two opposing positions: the Mu'tazila and the Ash'ari refutation.
The passage ends with a recap of the key principle.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila position
Find the condition the Mu'tazila place on God's infliction of pain. Why do they require compensation or offense?
3
Understand the Ash'ari refutation
The author argues that injustice is impossible for God, and nothing is obligatory on Him. Therefore, every act is either justice or bounty.
This removes the possibility of unjust infliction of pain.
4
Reflect on the implications
Why does the Mu'tazila view lead them to require retaliation among animals? How does this contrast with Ash'ari thought?
This illustrates the systematic difference between the two schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: ومن فروعه أيضا وهو أن الله إيلام الخلق وتعذيبهم من غير جرم سابق ولا ثواب لاحق خلافا للمعتزلة حيث لم يجوزوا ذلك إلا بعوض أو جرم وإلا لكان ظلما غير لائق بالحكمة، ولذا أوجبوا أن يقتص لبعض الحيوانات من بعض. انتهى. وقد سبق أن الظلم في حقه تعالى محال، وأنه سبحانه لا يجب عليه شيء بحال، ففعله إما عدل وإما فضل.
He said: And among its branches also is that God inflicts pain and tortures creation without previous offense nor later reward, contrary to the Mu'tazila, as they did not permit that except with compensation or offense, otherwise it would be injustice unbefitting wisdom, and therefore they obligated that He exact retaliation for some animals from others. End. And it has preceded that injustice with respect to Him is impossible, and that nothing is obligatory upon Him in any case, so His act is either justice or bounty.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage discusses a subsidiary issue: whether God can inflict pain on His creation without any prior sin or later reward. The Mu'tazila deny this, arguing it would be unjust and unwise, and they insist on compensation or prior offense. They even extrapolate that God must exact retaliation among animals. The author refutes this by restating the Ash'ari principle that injustice is impossible for God, and that nothing is obligatory upon Him. Hence, any act of God is either justice (if He punishes for sin) or bounty (if He gives without merit). There is no third category of injustice.

Key Terms
إيلام
جرم
عوض
عدل
فضل
ظلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God is obligated to be just in a human sense, and thus cannot inflict pain without compensation.
Correction: Ash'aris maintain that nothing is obligatory upon God; His actions are not subject to human standards of justice. All His acts are either justice (punishment for sin) or bounty (gift without merit).

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, without what can God not inflict pain?
A) Prior sin or compensation
B) Divine wisdom
C) Prior knowledge
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
God's actions are never unjust; He may inflict pain without sin or compensation, as nothing is obligatory upon Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God can inflict pain on you without any sin on your part, does that make you fear Him more or trust Him more? Why?
?
How does the Ash'ari principle that nothing is obligatory upon God challenge your sense of entitlement to comfort or justice in this life?
?
When you experience unwarranted hardship, do you instinctively blame yourself or others? How does this lesson correct that reaction?
